THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps (of any province) equal to the price stated
(which is published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
Some Books Worth Reading.
In the Midst of Life, by Ambrose Bierce,
Boards, 2s 6d. Some wonderfully fine
short stories.
The White Company, by Conan Doyle,
Paper, 2s lOd. A good historical novel.
Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes, by Conan
Doyle. Paper, 2s lOd.
Adventures of Sherlock Holmes, by Conan
Doyle. Paper, 2s lOd.
A Study in Scarlet, by Conan Doyle. Paper,
Is 3d.
The Sign of Four, by Conan Doyle. Paper,
2s lOd. First-class detective stories.
The Woman in White, by Wilkie Collins.
Paper, 9d ; Cloth, Is. The author’s
best book, well printed.
The Cloister and the Hearth, by Charles
Reade. Paper, 9d ; Cloth, Is.
Tess of the D’Urbervilles, by Thomas Hardy,
Paper, 2s lOd. A masterpiece of English
fiction.
Crime and Punishment, by Fedor Dostoieff-
sky. Paper, 3s. One of the most re-
markable of Russian realistic novels.
The Anarchist Peril, by Felix Dubois,
Cloth, 6s 6d. A startling exposure.
Lorna Doone, by R. D. Blackmore. Cloth,
Bs. A charming English romance.
Looking Backward, by Edward Bellamy.
Paper, Is 3d. The bane or the antidote.
Pictures of the Socialistic Future, by Eugene
Richter. Paper, Is 3d. The antidote or
the bane.
Geoffry r-amlyn, by Henry Kingsley.
Cl 3th, 4s. A readable tale about Australia.
Studies in Pessimism, by Schopenhauer,
Paper, Is. 3d. Delightfully cynical.
The Green Carnation. Paper, 4s. ; cloth,
6s 6d. Witty and amusing.
The Human Species, by Prof. A. de Quatre-
fages. Cloth, 5s 6d.
Virginibus Puerisque, by R. L. Stevenson.
Cloth, 6s. 6d. Essays perennially charm-
ing.
My Lady Nicotine, by J. M. Barrie. Cloth,
6s. 6d. A book chief among ten thousand
and altogether lovely.
In a Canadian Canoe, by Barry Pain. Cloth,
4s Sketches, verses and stories varying
in merit, but with keen thought and a
clever style at the back of all.
Prose Fancies, by Richard Le Gallienne.
Cloth, 6s. 6d. A recent book of idyllic
essays.
Post-Prandial Philosophy, by Grant Allen.
Cloth, 4s. Readable essays.
The Autocrat of the Breakfast Table. The
Professor at the Breakfast Table. The
Poet at the Breakfast Table. By Dr.
O. W. Holmes. A beautifully-printed
edition ; neatly cloth-bound, 2s. lOd. each.
Also, pocket size, paper covers, 9d. each.
The Manxman, by Hall Caine (cloth, 45.).
A really fine novel, epic in breadth and
intensity.
The Tales of John Oliver Hobbes (including
“Some Emotions and a Moral,” “A Study
in Temptations,” “A Bundle of Life,”
and “The Sinner’s Comedy ”). Piquant
and clever. A charming edition, with
portrait of author ; cloth, 6s. 6d.
Aut Diabolus Aut Nihil and other stories.
Cloth, 4s, Clever and strong.
A Child of the Age, by Francis - Adams.
Cloth, 4s 4d.
Kenelm Chillingly, by Bulwer Lytton. His
best novel; cloth, 4s Id (recommended) ;
also, cloth, 2s 6d,
John Bull and Co. Cloth, 3s. lOd. Max’s
new book about Australasia and South
Africa.
Esther Waters ; cloth, 6s. 6d.
Under the Red Robe, by Stanley Weyman.
Paper 2s lOd.
A Gentleman of France, by Stanley Wey-
man. Paper, 2s lOd.
My Lady Rotha, by Stanley Weyman.
Paper, 2s lOd.
Three clever and interesting new historical
novels.
Thomas Carlyle’s Works.
The new edition of Carlyle’s works can be
strongly recommended. The books are
splendid value, being well bound, clearly
printed, and fit to stand in any library.
Portraits and maps are included. Cloth,
3s. Id. per vol. ; or the set of 20 vols.,

Translations from Musseus. Tieck, and
Richter, 1 vol.
The Permanent Force.
These are books deserving, in Bacon’s

Socialism, by Graham. Cloth, 5s 6d. The
best book on the subject.

The best popular monograph.

Brilliant and fascinating.

A BIG BIZ!
Our Trade is Big I Our Store is Big 1
Our Ideas are Bigl
We get bigger every day; on Saturday night
we are bigger than on the previous Monday,
and on the Wednesday following we are bigger
still. We can’t help growing. We have all the
elements of growth. The soil we are planted in
is good, the climate propitious, the people kind.
We are continually watered by the lifegiving
showers of a nation’s approval. Can anyone
wonder that we grow ?
Our growth is a wonderful thing. People
describe it as magical. There is no magic in it.
Anyone, or any set of people, can do the same if
they do as we have done. But they don’t; they
can’t find the way, charge too much for the privi-
lege of doing business with them, and generally
and deplorably make a mess of the whole affair.
That’s the mischief.
Most People Like Us I All Believe in Us!
Not a Few Swear ly Us I
It’s cupboard love. We suit them, make their
money go a long way, and generally render the
path of life an easy one. There’s no sentiment
about it. It’s real biz, and no mistake. In that
way we are beloved throughout Australia, the
measure of affection each one bestows upon us
being gauged by the amount of benefit received.
The more you buy at our store the greater your
liking for us will become, because the more you
invest with us the better dividends you receive.
If living IN THE COUNTRY, send for our
Catalogue and Patterns of anything you require;
if in town, call every day.

‘A GOLDEN SHANTY.
Kaleidoscopic Politics .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
As broken glass reflects the rays
Of passing light a thousand ways.
And offers to the gazer % view
An ever-changing form and hue,
To one fast law no instant true,
So moves that mass of shams and tricks—
Kaleidoscopic politics.
Strange ! one straight truth can never reach
Its destined goal in human speech ;
But, biassed by the mind’s condition,
Is twisted out of recognition,
And, like a turbid stream, exudes
In dull and endless platitudes,
That gurgle o’er the suff’ring ground
To swell the awful sea of sound.
We’re weary of the ass’s bray—
The little dog that barks all day,
The hound that nightly bays the moon )
O Lord ! in mercy send them soon
To that bright corner of the skies
That Satan viewed—Fools’'Paradise.
We’re sick of the undying screech,
The dreadful and eternal speech,
Of brazen cheek and leathern lung,
Of thorax tough and ceaseless tongue
That’s most tenacious in the wrong,
Arid on this earth has wagged too long.
Amid the din a nation’s prayer
Arises on the tortured air :
“ O, mighty Author of the spheres
Give peace unto our suffering ears,
And with Thy lightning dart transfix
These conjurors in politics,
Who cackle o’er the empty theme,
And brooding sit on addled scheme.
O Lord ! the voice of Chaos cries,
Plant some sure symbol in the skies,
And, ’mid the dark, infernal din
Let some unbroken ray stream in.
W.B.N.
William Turner, who suicided in Melb.
the other day because his sweetheart had joined
the Salvation Army, gave full and particular
orders for his coffin and funeral before walking
into the Yarra. Very considerate conduct, but it
has its drawbacks. How unpleasant to be sued
by your undertaker should you change your mind
before getting into the water, and not have the
cash to square up for your coffin !
The emphatic and useful little word,
Damn, according to the latest dictionary—Dr.
Murray’s—comes from an Indian root. In India
there used to be a small coin called a dawm, valued
at about the 1600th part of a rupee. When a
man says he “don’t care a damn,” he only means
that he doesn’t care a copper-coin worth that
fraction of a rupee. Pity that so good a word
should have so cheap an origin.
A Dublin Trinity College-man, after serv-
ing a term under N.S.W. Education Department,
wen* to Queensland as a teacher, and thence
graduated as agent, reporter, and actor to unem-
ployedism. Then one day he asked a rag-and-bone
picker for a job. The latter was also a man of
education, but he was educated in rags and bones,
not in classics. “Go to Hades,” he said, briefly ;
“ what do you know of my profession ?”
The Archbishop of Canterbury has found
a convincing reason why the Church of Wales
ought not to be disestablished. It is the oldest
portion of the Church of England, so old that the
modern Welshman, as a rule, is unable to name
an ancestor who professed to belong to it. And,
as regards tithes levied upon dissenters, the
Archbishop explains that tithes are not really
taxes in Wales, but a grant set apart hundreds
and hundreds of years ago. Their extreme age
ought to make ’em sacred, he thinks. A century
hence there would be nobody left in the Welsh
Church except the parson, but the “ Canterbury”
of 1995A.D. would plead all the more earnestly for
the dear old tithes, because they were another
hundred years older.
A once well-known municipal potentate,
who faded away out of the memory of p ople in
Victoria years ago and is generally supposed to be
dead, was heard of the otiier day. He runs wild
along with a tribe of blacks in the scrub regions
of the Coen (N.Q.). The ex mayor is dressed
chiefly in a sugar bag ; his hair hasn’t been cut
for some years; and when he pelts through the
scrub with his unkempt mane flying behind him
he is a fearsome spectacle. He is a hatless, shoe-
less being with a harem, and is about as aboriginal
as the aboriginals themselves. His wife and
family in Victoria draw the rents from !>is pro-
perty, and have done so ever since the old man
vanished in the N.Q. wilds,- and they are
likely to continue doing so undisturbed.
Eor the old man isn’t coming back. He is
quite contented roaming the scrub with his
harem, and his niggers, and his fellow-derelicts.
There are a good many wild and greasy wanderers
of his kind in Carpentaria, and most of them are
known vaguely as Bill, or something to that
effect. No man who disappears mysteriously in
Australia can be safely set down as dead until
the scrub-country has been raked for him, and it
is definitely proved that he isn’t there. Some
of the more recent mysteries of Austra-
lasia, and a good many of ancient date, might
possibly be cleared up by a careful search in the
regions of Cape York Peninsula, or thereabouts.
A good many brown, neglected-looking waifs and
hermits and odd Calibans of these parts', if
they were caught and washed by the strong arm
of the law, would be found to be persons with a
history attached to them.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Maclbod, Managing Director.
Biter ary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald!.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
* Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other A rticles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Betters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible foar its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Slack and White,
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion — i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will he
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also :— R. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.
Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, Queensland
and South Australia,
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage by the Australian and Tasmanian Governments
on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual subscription
outside N.S. Wales is now £l, with 2s. 2d. added for
postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

SATURDAY, MARCH 16, 1895.
The Federal Balance-Sheet.
A century ago, or thereabouts, there lived
one Sieves, who made constitutions for
the first French Republic. He supplied
the country with quite a number of them in
his time, and he generally kept two or three
new constitutions on hand to be ready for
any emergency. They were exceedingly
beautiful constitutions, all of them, and,
theoretically, almost perfect, and under each
successive constitution the value of the
assignats kept falling, and the Treasury kept
growing empty, and the army became more
hungry and shoeless than under the one
before. The moral of which is that the most
symmetrical constitution ever invented can do
very little good by itself, and Australia,
which has been busy for some time past,
officially and unofficially, in drafting experi-
mental constitutions for the Commonwealth
that is to be, would do well to recognise this
fact. So far, experience has shown that it
is comparatively easy to draft a constitu-
tion —or half -a - dozen constitutions—for
Federated Australia, and exceedingly difficult
to get Australia to federate under any of
them. Of the obstacles in the way, the
first and, at present, the most trouble-
some —the indifference of the masses—
will doubtless remedy itself in course
of time. The opposition of the Fat Man
Governments of Queensland and Westralia
will also remedy itself when the Fat Man
dies of his own excessive girth. Even the
provincial jealousies over the division of
power in the Federal Parliament may not
be an absolutely hopeless element. But the
most unpromising feature in the case at the
present moment is that politicians in all the
provinces have begun to figure up the assets
which each province will bring into the
Commonwealth, and the liabilities which it
will shuffle off on to its neighbors, and to
publish estimates according to which it
appears that each writer’s own particular
province will be badly defrauded when its
assets and liabilities are pooled under
Federal control. And, as the most beautiful
constitution which the brain of man can
devise will furnish no means by which the
provinces can combine on terms of absolute
financial equality, all this balancing of
accounts looks unpromising.
• # *
No person in his senses, who had seriously
considered the question, ever supposed that
Federation could be arrived at on purely
equitable terms. If the Commonwealth is
to start by each province putting exactly the
same proportion of capital into the partner-
ship, then there will never be any Common-
wealth. If union is to be postponed until
some scheme shall be devised by which each
province will furnish exactly the same
revenue per head of population as every
other province, then there will be no union
in our time, or in the time of our sons, or
our grandsons, or at any other measurable
period. The Commonwealth must take over
the debts of all the individual provinces, and
some of the more heavily-indebted States
will thereby shuffle off a portion of their
burdens on their neighbors. It must take
over the railway systems, which bear no sort
of proportion to each other in value, and
probably the public lands, which are also a
variable quantity, and it will impose a
common tariff, which will produce a much
larger revenue per head in some provinces
than in others. Then, whether it returns
all surplus revenue to the Provincial Govern-
ments in proportion to the amount contri-
buted by each province, or in proportion to
population, or in any other proportion, it
will—as can be shown by figures—inflict a
serious injustice in any case on one or other
of the States. All of this, no doubt, is very
regretable, but Federation is not an exact
science, and theoretical injustice is unavoid-
able.
* * «
Taking the public debt as the first and
most serious item in the account, this varies
from £74 per head in Queensland down to
about £4O per head in Victoria. The
average rate is a little over £5O per
head, so the immediate result of Federa-
tion would be that each Victorian would
be saddled with a further liability of
£lO per head, each Westralian with a
liability of £6 per head, and each inhabitant
of New South Wales with a liability of
£2 10s. per head, while a resident of South
Australia would be relieved of a burden of
£l5, and each Queenslander would profit to
the extent of £24. The position of Tas-
mania would be practically unaltered. This
would be the immediate result, but with the
more rapid increase of population in the
large provinces of New South Wales and
Queensland it is quite probable that, 20
years hence, Victoria might be profiting con-
siderably at their expense. In proportion to
population Queensland has the largest State
railway system ; then follow South Aus-
tralia, Western Australia, Tasmania and
Victoria, with New South Wales a very bad
last. The cost of construction varied from
£14,743 per mile in New South Wales and
£12,665 in Victoria, through varying degrees
of extravagance down to £4441 per mile in
Westralia, and there is no sort of proportion
between the debt to be handed over to the
Federal Government and the railway assets
which accompany the liability.
• * •
The difference in the revenue from rail-
ways is another serious discrepancy. In
N.S. Wales and South Australia the railways
almost pay working expenses and interest on
capital expended, and if the Federal Govern-
ment consolidates the aggregate public
debt into one uniform 3 per cent, stock,
which should be easy of accomplishment, the
railways of these two provinces would pay a
handsome dividend. Under the same sys-
tem the Victorian lines would about pay
their way in the present, and would also
yield a profit in the future. But the lines of
Tasmania and Westralia are worked at an
enormous loss, and those of Queensland are
also very unprofitable. With the debt
placed on a 3 per cent, basis, the whole rail-
way revenue of Australia, according to the
latest returns, would just cover expenses and
interest, and thus iTasmania would shift its
burden on its neighbors and give nothing in
return for this assistance, and Queensland
would shift its railway deficit and one-third
of its debt as well on to the other provinces.
• * •
If the Federal Government takes over the
public lands of the various provinces, there
is again no sort of equality. Tasmania,
which would gain slightly by handing over
its public debt, and enormously by transfer-
ring its unprofitable railway system, would
hand over by far the smallest asset in land.
South Australia, which would lose heavily
by handing over its lines, would give up to the
central Government perhaps the most valu-
able property of all. Westralia would con-
tribute an immense area of unsold desert.
Victoria would surrender the least of all the
continental provinces. Considering, how-
ever, the differences in value per acre and
other circumstances, it is impossible to form
any accurate estimate, except in the case of
Tasmania, which would obviously come off
best in the deal. Possibly, however, public
lands will be left in the hands of the pro-
vincial Government, so that this item may
not appear in the balance-sheet of the Com-
monwealth.
» • •
As already mentioned, the Federal Govern-
ment would almost certainly be able to
convert the Australian public debt into a
uniform 3 per cent, stock. By this means
all the provinces would profit largely,
but South Australia, Westralia and Tas-
mania would profit especially through the
superior credit of New South Wales,
Victoria and Queensland. The aboli-
tion of border customs duties w'ould also
involve a sacrifice which would be very un-
equally distributed. By the transfer of the
posts and telegraphs to the Commonwealth
Government, South Australia would hand
over a profitable business and take a share in
the losses of the other provinces, and Vic-
toria, where the department makes only a
small loss, would suffer considerably by
amalgamating with Queensland, Westralia,
and Tasmania. Victoria would surrender a
very large excise revenue, and New South
Wales one somewhat smaller, while the
other provinces would contribute almost no-
thing under this head. Until the conditions
of the various provinces assimilated, South
Australia would supply twice as much Cus-
toms revenue per head as Victoria, and
three times as much as Tasmania. Victoria,
as the principal manufacturing province of
Australia, would profit most by the abolition
of internal customs duties, and the establish-
ment of a uniform tariff against the outside
world. Also in a number of other ways the
loss or gain by the unification of Australia
would be distributed in a most unequal
fashion, and in no single particular, how-
ever small, is it possible to suggest a method
by which Federation can be accomplished on
an absolutely equitable basis.
m • •
The object of all this is merely to
prove the uselessness of going into financial
details on such a subject. Federation can-
not be arrived at in any fashion which will
prevent every province profiting in some
way, for a time at least, at the expense of
its neighbours, neither is it possible to make
the gains and losses balance each other so as
to arrive at even a rough semblance of
equality. Therefore the only logical course
is to federate for the sake of making Aus-
tralia a nation, and to leave wholly out of
consideration the odd coppers of provincial
discrepancies. Likewise, Federation cannot
be arrived at in any way which will give each
province a mathematically correct proportion
of political influence, nor can the Federal
capital be located in every province at once,
and there isno guarantee that the Federal rail-
way department will be able to apportion ex-
pend! ture on future public works so as to give
each province its exact share, either accord-
ing to its necessities, or its population, or
its contributions to the Federal exchequer,
or on any other fixed principle whatever.
And, though the probabilities are that the
various discrepancies will gradually diminish,
this is merely a probability, and there is no
possible guarantee that they won’t become
worse instead of better. The economies to
be effected by consolidating the public debt,
the judiciary, the alleged military and naval
establishments, the railways, and many other
public departments will be very great —so
great that, in all probability, even the pro-
vince which loses most by the partnership
will be better off than it was before, but
even this isn’t absolutely certain. And,
under these circumstances, the only thing to
be done is to leave off adding up figures and
counting possible losses, and endeavoring to
arrive at an impossible balance, and to
federate without taking any thought for the
morrow. The advantage to be gained is
more than worth the sacrifice. No one can
tell exactly what the sacrifice in any given
case may be, but the advantage is worth it,
anyhow.
Australian Appeals at Law.
Does dog, then, eat dog ? Is there no honor
among sharks ? Here is lawyer G. B. Barton
arguing in February Cosmos that the idea of
appointing an Australian Judge to the Privy
Council “ seems too absurd to be seriously
entertained,” unless—here is a loophole-*-in the
case of “a retired Australian Judge who had
really distinguished himself by his general
powers of mind, as well as his legal ability.”
And then he grasps all Australia’s benches in
the hollow of his forensic palm and remarks
that they are but a pinch of dust, after all.
Considering the source, his admissions are
startling. “By no chance can Australia hope
to see for many years to come a race of J udges
fairly on a level with those of England.”
“Judicial appointments in Australia are too
often bestowed as the reward of political ser-
vices without sufficient regard to the public in-
terests.” “A vacant Judgeship is now the
lawful prize of an Attorney-General; but that
officer is rarely, if ever, the leader of the Bar
otherwise than ex-officio, because the leader of
the Bar cannot afford to become an Attorney-
General. Thus the Bench has long been, and is
apparently destined to be ” —are your Honors
listening?—“recruited from the second and
third ranks of the Bar—a state of affairs
which has never been seen in England,
and never will be.” Then Mr. Barton pro-
ceeds offensively to clinch his argument by an
illustration drawn from the ’9l case of R.
v. M‘Leod, when a man was convicted by a
N.S. W. Court of Quarter Sessions of bigamy
committed in the United States, without objec-
tion by the Supreme Court, to which the case
was subsequently taken. When defendant ap-
pealed to the Privy Council, the J udicial Com-
mittee quashed the case at once on the ground
that N.S.W. Courts had no jurisdiction to deal
with crimes committed abroad ; and the Judi-
cial Committee was thereupon severely lectured
for its ignorance by local Bar and Bench. All
this is very gratifying to Australian Judges,
and still more gratifying to the public, who in-
variably expect when lawyers fall out to get a
better grip of the justice which they call their
own.
• • *
But when Mr. Barton's arguments come to
be seriously considered, they have little force
for laymen. For example, in referring to the
Supreme Court of Australia constituted by the
Federal Constitution Bill of ’9l—on the
ground, as stated by Mr. John Downer, that
“ Australia has reached a stage of national
life in which we are fairly competent to manage
our own concerns, not merely political, but
judicial as well Mr. Barton’s comment is
that it seems singular that statesmen “ should
have shown themselves so ready to throw
away one of the fundamental rights and liberties
of the British subject ’’—meaning, apparently,
the right of appeal to the Privy Council. Pass-
ing the objection that the connection which
makes Australians British subjects is only a
temporary connection, inevitably to be set aside
sooner or later, Mr. Barton’s fallacy is
obvious. The “ fundamental right and liberty ”
of the British subject, so far as it has any
value, is not the right of appeal to the Privy
Council, but simply the right of appeal. The
right of appeal to CLesar was not cherished by
the Roman citizen simply because Caesar was
Caesar, and enjoyed ipso facto superhuman
wisdom. In creating an Australian Appeal
Court we do but exchange one for
another—a foreign Caesar for a Caesar at
home. Our “ fundamental right and liberty ”
of appeal subsists unchanged.
• • •
But Mr. Barton affirms that there are in
Australia no Judges fit to assume the responsi-
bilities of an Australian Appeal Court, and that
there will be none for a considerable time. “ A
degree of confidence is universally felt in the
judgments of the Judicial Committee which is
not met with in the case of our own Courts.”
Admitting, for argument’s sake, that this is so,
it is plain that, law technicalities apart, the
confidence is ill-founded. Mr. Barton naturally
judges from a lawyer’s point of view ; but, to
everybody not a lawyer, the exact interpreta-
tion of the letter of the law is of far less
moment than the just interpretation of its
spirit. The Judicial Committee is admirably
fitted to supply the one ; it is certainly incap-
able of giving the other. Accustomed to Eng-
lish conditions, saturated with English
precedents, what can it know of conditions
peculiar to Australia, of the precedents which
every day we are creating ? And who will con-
tend that justicecanbe done by a tribunal utterly
ignorant of the circumstances under which its
suitors appeal for justice—ignorant even of the
laws which should govern its decision ? For
the formidable mass of Australian legislation is
now being so rapidly increased that it is diffi-
cult even for lawyers on the spot to keep pace
with and assimilate it. Does any sane man
believe that the members of the Judicial Com-
mittee even attempt the task?
• « •
And, in spite of Mr. Barton, this incapacity
of the Privy Council in Australian cases is no
mere imagination. Greater confidence in the
judgments of the Judicial Committee, con-
sidered as a whole, is not felt by the Australian
public—probably not by the weightier half of
the Australian legal profession. There do
arise occasionally cases which hinge upon that
portion of the law of England which is practi-
cally unchanged in Australia ; and in these, no
doubt, the Judicial Committee’s* judgment is
more authoritative than would be that of any
Bench of Judges brought together in Aus-
tralia. But such cases are rare swimmers in
the vast whirlpool of appeals which impera-
tively demand for decision some knowledge—
often a great deal of knowledge—of Australian
laws and conditions. The attitude of the Judi-
cial Committee to such cases is simply pitiable,
as Mr. Barton well knows. Its members
have no choice but to pick up their knowledge
of law and facts from the addresses of counsel,
and, blinking feebly over their glasses, to apply
English precedents as far as (or farther than)
they will go, and plunge wildly into a decision.
• « •
Were there no alternative course, the ap-
pointment of an Australian Judge to instruct
this incapable committee would be absolutely
necessary. As there is an alternative course in
the creation of an Australian Appeal Court, the
“idea,” as Mr. Barton says, “is absurd”
not for the reasons hinted by Mr,
Barton, but because it is foolish to
send suitors to England to get what is,
on the whole, worse law and less justice than
the law and justice dispensed in Australia. It
is admitted that an Australian Appeal Court
would not be as perfect as it might be ; but
that is a defect which the lapse of time will
steadily remedy, while the unfitness of the
Privy Council to dispense justice to a self-
governing community of millions of souls,
16,000 miles away, must steadily increase.
And, even if an Australian Appeal Court w ere
much worse than it is likely to be, it is still in-
finitely to be preferred, on account of its local
knowledge and local training, to any foreign
court whatever. Mr. Barton errs in thinking
the public demand that the decisions of its
courts should tally exactly with the devious
course of English law since Edward the Con-
fessor. The public can do without accumulated
precedents as long as they get common sense
and a reasonable amount of equity applied to
the construction of Australian statutes. Let
English lawyers interpret English law : let
Australian law be decided by Australian
lawyers in Australia.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
B. R. Wise’s Amendment.
B. R. Wise, M.L.A., the benevolent theorist
of N.S.W. Assembly, came forward with a
theoretically-beautiful amendment on the Land-
Tax Bill, which amendment was promptly
rejected by a big majority. Mr. Wise’s theory
was that the State, when valuing land for taxa-
tion purposes, should exempt the amount ori-
ginally paid for it to the Treasury, and levy an
impost only on the additional value which it
has gained by increase of population, the build-
ing of roads and railways, and other circum-
stances. Thus if a man bought 10,000 acres


from the State at £1 an acre, and it is now
worth only 19s. 6d. per acre, it would be wholly
exempt from tax, while if another man bought
100 acres at the same price and it is now worth
JB3OO in all, he would be taxed on the £2OO of
added value. In other words, Mr. Wise wanted
to lay down the just principle that the unearned
increment belongs to the State, and to assert
the principle by taxing the unearned increment
accordingly. All this was strictly in accord with
Single-tax principles ; also, it was strictly in
accord with a certain kind of theoretical justice.
Still, the Assembly was distinctly justified in
Jumping on the proposal as it did, the only
pity being that the Assembly jumped on it
apparently from no good motive. To all
appearance it objected, not because it saw that
Mr. Wise’s theoretically perfect amendment
was all wrong in practice, but merely because
it was hot and flurried and in a bad temper,
and didn’t care much for justice, theoretical or
otherwise. Yet there wefe at least five good
reasons for its conduct, had it only known them.
(1) The small agricultural owner should be
exempt, if there is to be any exemption at all,
even though he has a large unearned increment,
and the large owner should not be exempt,
though his land is now worth less than lie paid
for it, simply because it is the policy of every
State to encourage small holdings. They lead
to better cultivation, and they create a large
class of independent agriculturists, whereas
large holdings create a small body of wealthy
owners and a vast, helpless, servile mass of
underpaid laborers. (2) The large holder often
has no unearned increment solely through his
own fault. Increment comes from population,
and, where half - a - dozen men shut out
the public from 500,000 acres of land,
they prevent any serious increase of population,
and reduce the value of the soil through their
own greed. Therefore, Mr. Wise proposes, in
such cases, to exempt the big holder because of
the injury he has done to the community. (3)
The small holder’s allotment is generally
increased in value because of the labor he has
; spent in improving it; the large grabber’s pro-
perty often fails to increase in value because he
jhas snatched more of the public estate than he
,can put to any profitable use. B. R. Wise
imakes no allowance whatever for this very
,obvious contingency. (4) The large grabber’s
[land deteriorates also, as a marketable asset, in
many cases for want of a convenient market
; and for want of railway communication, simply
because, by shutting out population, he makes
iit impossible that there should be a market,
and he keeps the country a desert so that there
iis no population to support a railway. And (5)
B. R. Wise’s statement that it isn’t the purpose
of the State “ to punish men for holding land,”
is partly correct and partly incorrect. If
he holds enough land for the comfort-
able maintenance of himself and family,
and puts it to a profitable use, there-
by enriching the country, he should not
be punished. If he holds it for purely specu-
lative purposes in order to block settlement and
force up prices by creating an artificial scarcity,
or for the purpose of letting it and living on a
rack-rented tenantry, or if he doesn’t put it to
profitable use, and merely, through indolence
or cussedness or some other cause, prevents
other people using it, to his own loss and that
of the country —in all these cases he should be
punished for holding land. The “unearned
increment,” as B. R. Wise uses the term, has a
comforting sound, but the nation can’t live on
sounds, and it is impossible to cure all the ills
, of the community with a single dogma, whether
it be Protection, Freetrade, Single- tax, Teetotal-
ism, or anything else. Therefore, the dogma-
, tist of one idea is only a dreary and useless
ibore.
The Importer’s liOgic.
Melbourne Argus, which has been taken
worse ithaaa usual of late over the tariff question,
Stad .another bad attack the other day, when it
attempted to define its faith as follows :
We fo*v t e itodwe toy our exports from the land ; we have
to sell our iproduce abroad. And our exports will be paid
for by imports. Ju the list twenty years we have ex-
ported produce ixova Victoria to the value of more than
£250,000,000. And every penny of payment for this pro-
duce has been made in (roods, and every penny of pay-
ment for the next £250,000,000 of produce we ship will be
made in goods also. . . Our toilers want the most they
can obtain for their produce, and they are not served,
but they are injured, when the high-tariff man steps in
with his assertion —“ The smaller the payment from
abroad the better,”
This beautiful piece of logic almost arouses an
idea that the Freetrade organ is off the remains
of its head, and has been putting an enemy in
the hinder-pocket of its pants to steal away its
brains. Who, or where, or what is that alleged
Protectionist who says that “ the smaller the
payment from abroad the better ?” If Victoria
sells goods in England, and takes English goods
instead of cash in return, it buys them at
English prices, and whether Victoria charges
100 per cent, duty on them or no duty at all,
the quantity it gets in England is exactly the
same—unless, indeed, the duty makes the seller
eut down his prices, and give more goods in
■ order to compete with the Australian manu-
facturer. Moreover, it is pure drivel to say
1 that every penny of payment for Victoria’s
exports, or any other country’s exports, comes
in goods ; a whole lot of it merely comes in the
: shape of the British money-lender’s receipt for
■the half-year’s interest, and the rest generally
■comes in goods, and if there is a balance against
■ us it is either paid in gold or else is liquidated
Iby exporting this country’s I O U. The mere
[fact that Britain buys from the United States
ito the extent of <£9i,000,000 annually, and only
:sends back goods to the value of £35,000,000,
is a solemn evidence that a country only takes
payment in goods when it wants to, and not
•otherwise. Also, the fact that Australia has
■accumulated a debt of several hundreds of
millions is another proof that goods aren’t
necessarily paid for with goods, for this country
has evidently bought some hundreds of
millions’ worth of goods which it hasn’t
been able to pay for, consequently it owes
the money to this day. And this country
still keeps on buying goods which it can’t
pay for with other goods, consequently the
debt keeps increasing, and the country keeps
hard-up, and the Argus keeps on making a
fool of itself by telling an impossible story about
an alleged state of things which never existed,
and never will exist. And, even if the Argus’
impossible theory could be true, it doesn’t
amount to much. If Victoria did live by selling
its raw material in London, and getting back
its exact value, less freight, manufacturers’
profits, wages, commission, exchange, &c., on
finished goods, why shouldn’t it live equally
well by selling its raw material to local manu-
facturers, and getting back its value in finished
goods from them, and thus save the freight and
commission and exchange, and keep the wages
and manufacturers’ profits in the country ? It
is all very good to bash down the dogma that
we “ have to live ” by our exports and get im-
ports in return ; but why have we ?
The Pitt Town Martyrs.
Were the judgments of the Labor press any-
thing more than a mixture of sentiment and
shriek, the five men cast out of Pitt Town
(N.S.W.) co-operative settlement might easily
be pedestailed as the Pitt Town martyrs.
Their condition in no way justifies such
canonisation. No doubt it was foolish of New
South Wales Legislative Council to insist on
the disenrolment of the single settlers. They
might have been left to sink or swim with the
married ones, seeing their numbers were so few,
and that, anyhow, a single man has quite as
much right to be supported by the State as a
married one— i.e., he has no right at all. For
the common preference of married men to single
ones as objects of beneficence involves a very
obvious fallacy. Certainly, a married man is
worth more to the State if he supports himself
and his family. But if he comes demanding State
soup, all his marriage means is that there are
so many more mouths for the spoon to go into.
Marriage, in such a case, simply implies that the
man, careless of responsibility, has treated him-
self to the pleasures of a wife and trusted to le
bon Dieu to feed her and the children. As le
bon Dieu, in practice, is simply the congrega-
tion of taxpayers, and as there is no earthly
reason, generally speaking, why one fellow
should be taxed to feed another fellow’s pro-
ducts, aid should be rather withheld from, than
given to, the reckless and thriftless married
man, were it not that we must take facts and
twins as they are, and that the bitter cry of the
outcast latter is not easily to be disregarded.
But the single man, the man who has pru-
dence and self-restraint enough not to bring
into the world mouths which he can-
not feed, should certainly not be victim-
ised on that account only ; and from
this point of view the single men of Pitt Town
have valid ground of complaint. From any
other point of view their complaint has no
ground whatever. What are the facts ? On
July 21 next the Pitt Town settlement will have
existed two years. To the end of last mouth
<£6778 cash had been expended upon 100
families. Not .£3OO had been earned. The
improvements—including settlers’ huts, fencing,
and other things, which have little or no value
when the settlement is disbanded—are esti-
mated as worth ,£5991, so that the labor of 100
men and a number of youths for 19 months has
failed to earn rations for themselves and
families. There is now no doubt whatever
that, as soon as the Government subsidy
stops, the settlement must stop too.
The five complainants have had for 19
mouths their daily bread —including, pre-
sumably, their share of the currants and
raisins, pills and painkiller, pipes and tobacco,
bird-seed and balsam and cocoa, into which
“ daily bread ” is translated at Pitt Town—
found for them by the hard-working community
outside the Arcadian fence, who toil, many of
them, 10 hours a day to help to supply the cash
which the strictly 8-hour Arcadians absorb
with such alacrity. How much longer do the
men disenrolled expect this kind of thing to go
on ? Martyrs, indeed ! The real martyrs are
the community, upon whom Parliament has
permitted a set of amiable nincompoops to foist
an ideal poor-house with unexampled possibili-
ties in the way of carraway-seed and cayenne
pepper. It isn’t wholly the fault of the men,
of course, that the Pitt Town failure is so
ghastly. But the sooner the country sets its
foot on the neck of the “simple, beautiful,
Arcadian system,” the sooner the men will
mend.
The Lav of Appeal.
A married lady-teacher recently sued Vic.
Education Department for six months’ salary,
in lieu of a “proper notice” to quit, and
obtained a verdict. The department fought
and lost this case on the recommendation of the
late Attorney-General. The present A.G.
advises an appeal to the Full Court, inasmuch
as the decision re one lady-teacher entitles a lot
more of them to claim salary, amounting to
some £2500 in all. Practically Attorney-General
Isaacs says : “ The Government of the State
has failed to obtain justice from its Supreme
Court, and we will carry our grievance to the
higher, or Full Court.” Anyway, that is how
the outside world must interpret the meaning
of the department’s appeal. Supposing the
higher Court confirms the other (humorously
called Supreme) Court’s decision, ignor-
ant Victorians will assume that their
Attorney-General is an unsatisfactory law-
yer, because he altogether misunderstood
the law. If the Full Court confirms the opinion
of Isaacs by upsetting the original judgment,
the Supreme Court will stand condemned by
the Law, in the public eye, as a worse than
useless institution. Every appeal case excites
anew the bitter cry of common sense—What is
Law, and where is Justice bred ? To which a
sophistical barrister replies that the Law is
known to no one, and Justice is a matter of
opinion. Judges are mere blind men groping
for Truth in a dense fog of learned precedents
and modern instances. The sophistical bar-
rister refers, of course, to civil argumenta-
tive law. Men are hanged, flogged and
imprisoned by criminal law, and the wigs
all argue that justice is being administered.
There are no appeals against the gallows, or
the cat. The Judge sums up in a prisoner’s
favor, or otherwise, and a jury, more often than
not, forms his Honor’s opinion into a verdict.
Criminal law Justice is largely bred in a Judge’s
skull, but as regards Justice in civil law’, we
have the ghastly fact that civil lawyers always
differ as to its birthplace, and never feel certain
that it has been born, or even conceived. Laws
are made, Acts of Parliament passedj bar-
risters argue over them, Judges come to
decisions upon those arguments, after all which
—an appeal to another court. And, although
finality in legal judgment be arrived at, the
sophistical barrister does not admit that justice
is necessarily there. For nobody knows the Law.
The game of appeals and costs is based on this
tricky argument. A shameful game, surely.
Selfish litigants bring their honestly warped
notions, or impudently absurd pretences of
right and wrong, fairness and unfairness, to a
law-court. Yet one or two judges are held in-
capable, by the law of appeal, of deciding what
is right and fair according to Law. For no
lawyer knows the Law. Laymen, let it be
carefully observed, don’t desire to have the
Law’s identity wrapped in this expensive robe
of mystery. They support, in Victoria, six
judges, and, considering that any six judges are
bound to be always contradicting one another,
and any one of them is likely to know as much
as another, the public would be glad to have
each Court’s decisions made final. Men box, or
row for big stakes, and the umpire awards the
money upon a foul. The finality of his judg-
ment has been determined by written agree-
ment beforehand. There is no appeal against
the opinion of the man at the little window
who thinks the Melbourne Cup was won by a
horse that, perchance, finished a nose behind
the “ second ” animal. If the Great Unknown
Law were honestly desirous of settling legal
contests in the most satisfactory way possible,
it would require suitors to sign a document
binding themselves to abide by the first Judge’s
judgment they could obtain. Also the Law
would give every Judge the power to make his
jury’s decision final, if he deemed it wise and
fair. In other words, the Law would elevate
its group of supreme officers to the same dignity
as is enjoyed by the trustworthy individual who
umpires at a prize-fight.
“The Czar,” it is cabled, “has partially
abolished the use of the knout, restricting its
application to the discretion of the country
Judges.” On the other hand, New South
Wales Legislative Council now seeks to revive
the local use oi the knout—restricting its appli-
cation to the discretion of town and country
magistrates—a strong percentage of whom are
ignorant and malicious savages, as often drunk
as sober. Even some of the police-magistrates,
in towns, actually sit on the bench while drunk,
and quite a number of them can’t pull their
boots on any morning until they have had about
six whiskies. However, that doesn’t much
matter, as no black-coated or well-connected
youth or man has ever been, or ever will be,
flogged. The lash for the workman —and his
sonT The House of the Glutton’s Snore will
no doubt pass the new flogging-bill with
alacrity, but let every democratic voter mark
well the name of every member of Assembly
who dares to vote for a law which involves the
flaying of “ common people ” without the inter-
vention of a sober Supreme Court Judge and
jury.
The Inspector-General of New South Wales
police, in his report for ’94, says that the
“Sunday-closing clause” of the Licensing Act
is “no better observed by licensees than in
previous years.” Let the boot be put on the
right foot. The licensees don’t observe the
clause simply because the public won’t observe
it. Isn’t it about time that a little common-
sense was imported into legislation, and that
laws that are absolutely repugnant to public
opinion should be repealed or at least re-
modelled ? The Inspector-General actually
declares in his report that it is found impractic-
able “ without neglecting other important
duties ” to employ a sufficient number of men
to enforce this law. Until the teetotallers can
make the public conscience believe that Sunday
beer is sinful as well as illegal, the public will
insist on buying and drinking its Sunday beer.
When the teetotallers do achieve that result
(in the Greek Kalends, of course), the public
will admit the sin also of week-day beer, and
will acquire teetotalism, gluttony and unchas-
tity as its trinity of strong points.
Sydney Truth sums up the reasons for not re-
engaging Commissioner Eddy at an increased
salary, with a holiday thrown in :
Lady Plagiary thus hits off the Eddy episode:—
“ Would I re-engage a cook at a rise in her salary, and
then allow her to go off to Paris (expenses paid) to learn
how to make some new dishes ? No, Sir Fretful, I would
not— and well you know it.” “ And well I no know it 1”
In other words, Eddy is only a sort of appren-
tice, and he has gone to Europe and America to
study his business at this country’s expense,
whereas what New South Wales wants is a
finished railway expert who knows all his
business already. Which wmuld be logical
if N.S.W. could find a railway expert who
knows everything in his own line that has been
invented up to date, and who also knows every-
thing that is going to be invented during his
life-time. But, unluckily, there is no such man
alive just now. Every year Europe and America
are turning out new labor-saving and money-
saving appliances for railway purposes, and if
the railway systems of Australia are to be kept
up-to-date one of three things must happen; (1)
Either a clever official must make a peri-
odical tour to inspect the latest discoveries,
and see them at work, and adopt those that are
worth adopting ; or (2) this country must pur-
chase all the new inventions indiscriminately,
and find out at its own cost whether they are
good or bad ; or (3) Truth must arrange that
all such new apparatus shall hereafter be
invented in Australia and nowhere else, so that
they will always be on the spot. As an exam-
ple, the Vic. Railway Commissioners recently
discovered that they could save yearly an
amount, variously stated at from £16,000 a year
to almost double that sum, by the use of a cheap
little machine which oils the engine automa-
tically, instead of having the refreshing fluid
poured on by hand. One small discovery like
that is worth four times Eddy’s salary, and it
is worth paying a man to travel round the
world in search of similar trifles.
When is New South Wales going to establish
an information bureau in connection with the
Lands Department ? Said bureau should con-
tain maps of every land district in the
province, with all the land available for
selection accurately and accurately marked
thereon. Similar maps shouid be avail-
able at important country towns such as
Newcastle, Goulburn, Albury, Bathurst, and
others, and, in addition, every country lands
office should have an accurate map of its
own district marked in the same fashion,
These should be kept always well up to
date, and any official whose map wasn’t
corrected and revised, up to within a
fortnight at the outside, should be promptly
and enthusiastically sacked. Also, the depart-
ment should be able to supply information
about roads, markets, population, soil, and
kindred subjects, and any official who didn’t
keep himself posted up in all necessary facts of
this kind should be either discharged or turned
into a policeman. At present, such informa-
tion can only be obtained from private agents
who charge a small fee of <£2o or so for
finding the applicant a 100 acre selection, and
whose business is mostly a ghastly compound of
roguery, swindle and misrepresentation.
Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insuffi-
ciently stamped. Name and address must
appear on MS. No liability is undertaken re
contributions voluntarily submitted, and in no
case will MS. be returned unless stamps ac-
company.

G. (Sydney): You say that according to Bullbtin logic

last statement is that most wealth comes from income.
This paper supports both Land and Income tax. It never
said that land wasn't a source of wealth. Also, the par.
referred distinctly to wealth derived from the land, and
took no account of the immense value of land as standing-
room for humanity. The land on which London is built
has an enormous artificial value, but the wealth you get
from it in vegetable or mineral products is practically nil.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of Careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by .skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the lie,Ring properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

My Dear Moorabinda—
Mayoress Lees’ “first Tuesday” at Sydney
Town Hall on sth inst. was not a supreme sort of
outbreak—it looked like a weird ceremony for
women only, since scarcely a man relieved the
weary waste of petticoats. It is significant that
the women follow men into smoking-rooms and
dare the most horrible mixed tobacoodors in
order to never let Adolphus give ’em the slip—yet
men (I mean real men, not tame cats) won’t brace
up to one small cup of afternoon-tea in order to
bask in the society of women. That’s another
point on which the sexes are not equal.
A change came o’er the spirit of the music at
Mrs. Lees’ At Home—Miss Lees performing
selections on the great organ with pleasant effect.
She is the second of Wiegand’s pupilesses who has
tackled the big instrument, and the result made
me think that a lady should always be laid on to
play for occasions when people want to talk.
Under the srnitings of Wiegand, the organ tyran-
nises over the tympanum, but beneath a woman’s
hand it only growls sotto-voce, like a departing
thunderstorm.
Mrs. Lees and her daughters wore albescent gar-
ments of simple make—most sensible wear for a
very hot day, and the Lenten season hadn’t en-
couraged a rush of sackcloth and ashes to the
figure on the part of the guests, who all suggested
in their attire that the wardrobe was almost
empty at home. Some of the new initial hat-pins
were on view. I can’t say I really like them.
People whose initials suggest the points of the
compass ought not to wear them, anyhow, unless
they want to look like weather-vanes. Fancy a
girl with a big “S ” on one side of her skull and
a huge “W” on the other! Nobody could help
watching her to see if the wind was changing.
These immutable “Thirty-second Wednesdays,”
and so forth, on the part of official women, to
which Mrs. Tom, Dick, and Harry make a point
of going, are very often used by people to keep
their friends off their carpet and to effect economy
of refreshments. I mean that Mrs. Blank im-
pressively invites a regular round robin of her
friends to meet her at Mrs. Official Personage’s
heterogeneous “At Home.” When there they
mass into a coterie, Mrs. Blank taking the lead,
and, after a chat, Mrs. Blank suggests an adjourn-
ment for tea, just as she would in her own house.
Calm, isn’t it ? The most they do to the hostess
of the afternoon is to simply pump-handle her on
entering, and they will dodge out of any side-door
to avoid formal farewells. Yet somebody asked
at the Sydney “Literary” Women’s Club, the
other day, “ What is a vulgar person ?”
The engagement is announced of Miss Garran
(the “Miss Wilmot ” of the B. and B. Co.) to Mr.
B. Harper, of Manly Beach.
Mr. Churchill Otton managed the complimen-
tary concert tendered to ’cellist Gerard Yollmer at
Sydney Y.M.C.A. last week. Herr Yollmer
means to make his camp in Sydney as a ’cello
tutor, and though I have not detected a burning
anxiety on anybody’s part to learn the large, loud
instrument, still I have no doubt mein Herr has
received due encouragement, or surely he would
never have joined the throng of musicians who
are like sands on the shore in Sydney. Nellie
Young, in blue brocade, kindly lent her creamy
voice to grace the occasion ; and Henri Stael,
Kowalski, and Mr. Sykes helped things to an
artistic conclusion. By the way, 1 was much
struck by a hat I saw at the concert. It was a
longish black hat, and out of the top of the high
crown burst a quantity of flaming flowers. This
ought to be called the chimney-a-fire effect.
Quite the latest London society craze is the New
Baths Club (to build it cost .£80,000), in which
Australia has some sort of an interest, since the
club is installed at the residence of the papa of one
of the imminent Lord Brassey’s aides-de-camp.
The subscription is altitudinous, and the rnembah-
ship select, don’t you know. The place contains
every sort of baths —Turkish, Russian, and all-in
addition to the ordinary conveniences of a first-
class club, and only 550 membahs, the very top-
cream of London, will disport in the baths —a
description of which reads like the last days of
Pompeii. In faot, anybody who laves in these
hallowed waters is socially baptised for evermore
(bar accidents). Being dipped in Jordan is nothing
to it.
There is a ladies’ day, too, when all the best
women (or those not too frescoed to stand the
ordeal) will disport like Naiads in the marble
baths, and scratch their names with diamond rings
on the sides thereof, and, taking it altogether, it
seems a pity that the New Baths Club didn t start
when “ Ouida” was at her palmiest, for the whole
\hing appears to be much in her vein. The idea
is a splendid one for this climate.
Maoriland had a vice regal sensation, last week,
when Lady Augusta Boyle slipped off a bareback
steed and fractured her leg at the ankle. It is, en
parenth&se, very clever of Lady “ Gussie ” to
break it at the respectable ankle. “ How is your
ankle?” is a permissible query from either sex —
whereas “How is your leg?” would never do.
Bare-back riding is a new vice-regal departure
in Australasia, and is a frolic that no tame young
lady ought to attempt. Certainly the bush-girl
rides as well on an empty chaff-bag as on the best
hogskin, but the tame-girl doesn’t bestride her un-
dressed steed bush-girl fashion—she humps her
knee over the gee’s wither as if it was a crutch,
and either tumbles clear over the off-side or slides
down the near. Unless a girl is prepared to have
a foot on either side, bare-back riding is to her a
sealed sport.
Mrs. Brough’s farewell ben., at Sydney Lyceum,
drew a flatteringly full house, and the baskets and
bouquets that went over the floats turned the stage
into a flower-show when Mrs. B. spoke her
valediction. By the way, one clothes-basket full
was posted near the front, and the donor’s card
with its pencilled sentiments was turned full on
the public, who read it through their opera-glasses.
It must be a creepy sensation to write a senti-
mental message with your name attached to an
actress, and sit by while hundreds of unsym-
pathetic people read it, too.
I liked Mrs. Brough’s farewell speech better
than the last one, which was too calmly succinct
to be convincing. After all, it is a very much
more real thing to say good-bye before going ’Ome
than to make second-class cm revoirs before pro-
ceeding on a professional tour round Australasia.
Mrs. Brough was artistically nervous in her
a lieux at the close of her nine years’ campaign on
the ’Stralian stage. I suppose practice makes
you perfect in farewell-speechifying as in every-
thing else. I thought Mr. Brough’s “spoken”
was a perfect gem in stage speeches-flimsy enough
when viewed in cold blood, but just the necessary,
airy bit of persiflage which cleared the atmosphere
of heroics. “I am an old friend of Mrs.
Brough’s,” he said, and everybody laughed.
In spite, though, of flowers and fuss, I do not
think the charming Mrs. Brough was so univer-
sally adored as she might have been had not
Mr. B. sternly (and very sensibly) decreed that
she must not recite even “ Casabianca ” for the
edification of small tea-parties, the givers of which
are generally prone to forget what a breach of
professional etiquette it is to ask artists to sing
for their suppers. Mrs. B. never performed gratis
(bar benefits, mind you), and the only time in nine
long years she ever gave the public a cheap show
(unless my memory plays me a trick) was last
Saturday, when she took a stall at a fair in aid of
a suburban Infants’Home (where one of the boy
babies is called “Robert,” after Mr. Brough, who
happened to be there when the bantling was dis-
covered on the mat), and dispensed useless bazaar
articles in the sacred cause of the “ foundlings.”
In another way, too, Mrs. Brough baffled the
local lioness-hunters, who yearned to make a sister
of her, and exhibit her in a tame state —she had no
“day ”at home; and so whole scurries of inquisi-
tive females, foolishly seeking to state their more-
or-less genuine admiration for the actress with
maudlin gushings over the woman, were blocked
at the very outset. But perhaps her complete
devotion to the stage is one of the reasons why
Mrs. Brough has reigned so long, and why she will
be received with open arms on her return. Pro-
fessionals who display their wares gratis in private
drawing-rooms, and submit to be pawed in the
way of friendship by patrons of the dress-circle,
haven’t always the longest artistic life.
Some of the “Masqueraders” (the new and
improper play at Sydney Lyceum) reminded me
of some parts of the “ Heavenly Twins ” set to
music, as it were, but I liked the new leading
woman (leading lady is obsolete), Geraldine Olliffe,
who is indeed as an oliffe—l mean olive—in the
theatrical bill-of-fare. I don’t consider Miss O. a
professional beauty, for, though she has an emo-
tional profile, she is hollow-cheeked, and the con-
tour of her face is square ; but she is tall and
willowy, and possessed of a magnetism far beyond
that of the common electric-shock, and—she
knows how to dress herself. In the first act —a
“pink” ball at a country inn —the first part is
played in a black gown with a frizzy coiffure d la
Hebe, made realistic by a red bow, and the second
part in a yellow evening frock of suburban cut,
from which the chiffon comes off, so that Mr.
Elwood (he talks like Alfred Dampier) may pin it

In the second sob, Miss Olliffe sported a good-
looking dress of white-satin brocade, the train
lined with silk, and a folded bodice of pink and
huge white butterfly sleeves, a feather fan and a
stage-diamond necklace finishing off a very neat
toilette, which made the speechless portion of her
stage guests look more touzled than usual by con-
trast. Miss Bessie Tarrant (Miss Hammond)
looked crisp in a flesh-pink silk gown, but a semi-
amateur, who trailed in wearing a lavender dress
with what looked like a hearse-pall weirdly fes-
tooned at the back, was quite up to the sartorial
standard of the usual stage guest, and so was
another girl in a white, raggy sort of garment
with a bunch of scarlet flowers.
In the third act (after which the dressing
lets up) Miss Olliffe wore a shot-bean and reseda
silk with bodiceof puffed strawberry-colored chiffon
and a few effective patches of black passementerie.
Emma Temple and Grace Noble wore dominoes,
and the marvellously agile Dot Boucicault
cut a quaint figure as a Pagliacci, covering
his fool’s dress with a black cloak lined
with scarlet which he managed as deftly as a
skirt-dancer. “The Masqueraders” (which I
consider a wicked play, because in the end the
good man looks such a fool) contains a few
smart cynicisms. “Marriage is the last insult
a man offers to a woman he respects,” made several
d.c. matrons ruffle their feathers and call it a
horrid play.
I am not fascinated with Elwood, who reminds
me of a Sydney Q. 0., and who never seems to
forget his hat. For example, in the hotel-scene,
when he enters in a hot hurry to see his fate once
more, lie naturally flung his hat on to a chair, and,
as it slid off (properties always act as if possessed
on a first night), he most unnaturally stooped to
pick it up again. No matter how arduously a
man may act afterwards, a lover who is so con-
cerned about his boxer necessarily impresses you
with the idea that his hat is of more importance
to-him than the lady. If I were a he-actor I
wouldn’t like to go down to posterity as a man
with a perfect genius for bringing his hat out of
the most agonising situations without a dimple in
its crown.
Under the heading of a “grand wedding,” a
Northern paper gives a mile-long account of a
recent Sydney marriage when a wealthy Israelite
led his bride under the chuppah. In this report
every article in the bride’s trousseau is described
down to the very last goffer in a familiar way that
certainly goes one ahead of the common or garden
style of trousseau-reporting. It is unique, and
deserves to be reproduced if only to encourage the
“ society ” pen-woman to dive beneath the surface
in future.
After describing acres of silks, satins, cotton
and rags, the reporter (who is masculine and
married, I hope) takes a header. “The lingerie
is of the finest lawn and cambric richly trimmed
with Valenciennes and Torchon laces, veining, and
other delicate embroideries, all exquisitely marked
with a monogram of three letters, and the gar-
ments are in sets of three and also in combina-
tions (!). In the make the American fashion is
followed in many. The knickerbocker portion
of the combinations have finely-pleated frills
edged with lace. The frills are open on the outer
side, and ttiis arrangement takes away the pres-
sure of the seam and prevents the tearing that so
often results in the old style. The night-
dresses are so handsomely trimmed that with a
silk lining in front they would make perfect
blouse bodices. In the chemises, many have a
full cambric sleeve—a ball-gown sleeve in minia-
ture —set in to the band of insertion which forms
the shoulder-strap. The puff is about six inches
deep, and is edged with lace like that above the
insertion. The exquisite needlework, in addition
to the fine quality of the fabrics and trimming,
makes the underlinen very attractive.” Dress
reporting is indeed becoming “ thorough.” How
proud and happy the bridegroom must be, feeling
that the public know all about his bride’s “com-
binations,” and how neatly her nightgowns fit
her 1
Gov. Duff is now said to be suffering from
“lingering jaundice,” and the weirdest reports
float up from the tight little island. For one
thing, the Y.R. telegrams said, the other day,
that H.E. had not slept, and could eat nothing,
but still he was “progressing favorably!” A
patient who can neither eat nor sleep is not a very
promising convalescent. However, everyone hopes
for the best, and trusts that the Governor will
soon be restored to health.
I am pleased to hear that the tickets for ex-
Mayor Manning’s complimentary banquet are
having a hot sale, and that the affair promises to
bo a huge success. There is talk of giving ex-
Mayoress Manning a reception a la Mrs. Bowden-
Smith after the banquet, and this also is a good
idea, for the kindly lady certainly merits the
compliment. Ex-Mayor Manning goes ’Ome
shortly with Miss Manning for a business-cwm-
holiday tour.
Sydney has a good heap of wild, unearthly
melody in store for it. The Metropolitan Lieder-
tafel leads off with a glorified sing-song with
women’s voices for the first time, and assisted by
nine first-class artistes, including Miss Florence
Esdaile, Miss O’Shannessy, Kowalski, Strauss
and Arthur Deane (who will be a first-class artist
some day). The Sydney Liedertafel follows on
27th with one of its sure-to-be-good outbreaks,
assisted by a new Sydney soprano, Lottie Hyam,
Allpress and Arthur Deane. On 28th the Phil-
harmonic enters the lists with “ Samson,” assisted
by Mr. and Mrs. Philip Newbury, Mrs. “ Char-
ley ’’Edwards, Hallewell and Mr. Wildigg. The
long-haired are in for a regular treat.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

Jones’ drama, “ The Masqueraders,” which
introduced the new Brough-Boucicault 00. at
Sydney Lyceum, is an unsatisfying affair, with
many good things in it, and many things which
are not bad, but are only rather slow. It ends no-
where in particular, chiefly because the author has
by that tune got it into such a state that it can’t
end anywhere else. The story covers four acts,
and can be told in as many sentences. A bright,
ambitious girl has been reduced to become a bar-
maid at a fashionable hotel, and, being loved by a
poor and dreamy man of science and a hog-like,
gilded baronet, she marries the baronet, as most
girls would. The baronet drinks away his fortune
all but 3s. 3d., his other assets being a daughter, a
blotched, full-moon countenance, a footballer’s ex-
pression, and a wife who is on the verge of insanity.
The dreamy astronomer becomes rich, and stakes
his pile against his old love and her child in a mid-
night game of cards, and wins. Then he takes
the stakes away to his home among the Alps, and
there the lady thinks better of it, and they part.
The dreamer, with his unsatisfied longing still upon
him, goes away to Africa, and no one knows what
becomes of him. The lady stays behind, and her
after-fate is a matter of conjecture. The baronet
is left insensible on the floor, with 3s. 3d. between
him and perdition, and the drama goes on and
leaves him. A dozen other characters come into
the story, but the drama goes on and leaves them
all. In which respect it resembles a hydrophobic
patient the writer once met, whose disease com-
pelled him to run, barking as he went, and who
caught hold of bushes as he ran to try to stop him-
self until someone would overtake him. But
every time he did it the bush was dragged out of
the ground, and at last he ran over the horizon
with his hands full of herbage, and left everybody
behind. Jones also runs and leaves all his
characters behind.
• • «
Jones’s drama is life-like and realistic
because it is an unfinished tale of misery, and this
world in general is miserable and unfinished.
Also, it contains one really artistic scene and one
patch of tragic intensity which should make the
fortune of a much worse production. It is never
altogether bad, and once it manages to be alto-
gether good, while on two or three occasions it
only escapes being excellent with great difficulty.
Miss Geraldine Olliffe, Mrs. Brough’s successor,
is an unalloyed success. She is tall, graceful, and
stately, with a bright, expressive face, a strong
musical voice, and a magnetic presence ; also, she
is a finished actress, and, as Jones’s heroine, she
is nearly half the drama. Most of the other half
is Titheradge. As the baronet with the foot-
baller’s expression Titheradge outshines even him-
self. The greater part of the time he is a slouchy,
sullen, sodden, leering ape, but in the scene where
he gambles for his wife and daughter and loses
them he is blotchy and rampant, and the
living picture of an ape in a state of crazy
excitement. Mr. Elwood, another new importa-
tion, makes a great hit in the same scene, but th-
rest of the time he is a stagey person with a stride
and a sombre drawl and a number of exhaustirm
attitudes. The Bulletin suspends its judgment
until it ascertains if the stride and the drawl
belong to Elwood or if he belongs to them, and
respectfully hopes for better things. Mr. Comp-
ton, a third new arrival, has a small part and
no definite opportunity, save the opportunity ol
letting the public know that he pronounces surely
as “shurley.” Miss Beryl Faber leaves a de-
cidedly good impression in a Quakerish part;
Miss Talbot—still another new attraction—does
sufficiently well in a very small character; and
Cecil Ward is a bright spot in Jones’ wilderness.
Providing Elwood doesn’t always stride ami
Compton doesn’t say “ shurley ” too regularly, the
new importations promise well on the whole.
Bjucicault, who engaged them after persomd
inspection, is a man of few histrionic mistakes.

The Lyceum dress-circle audience didn’t
talk half so much as usual on Saturday night, and
it now only converses about three times as much
as the d.c. at the Royal or Her Majesty’s. The
principal disturbance on Saturday was caused by
a female with the longest neck in Sydney, wl.i“
cackled incessaptly with the female with the
shortest neck in N.S.W., and by the stentorian
voices of two well-known newspaper men who
need to be killed and eaten.

Mrs. Brough’s farewell on Friday night was
as big a function as the Lyceum would hold. Tin-
departing favorite made her exit in “ Niobe,” ami
the Greek lady who haunted the house of Peter
Amos Dunn never appeared to more advantage
She also made a pretty little speech, which was
altogether successful, especially when she struggl- 11
with her rising tears towards the end, and began
to be troubled by the exuberance of the audience.
Brough also farewelled in a cheerful vein, amt
didn’t struggle with his tears, for he hadn’t any
about him. The senior member of the firm was
in a happy mood, and gladdened the audience with
the joyous assurance that he would return by-and
bye. About two tons of flowers were handed over
the footlights, and towards the end of the last act
the house of Dunn looked like a gorged conserva-
tory. “Niobe” lasted longer than usual because
the audience insisted on cheering in season and
out of season, and in various other places besides,
and altogether it was a great occasion.

Miss Florrio Ford had a benefit at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney) on Saturday, and thereafter
the theatre closed fpr a fortnight to give the lessee
a well-earned rest. It is so long since Mr. Rignold
&ad a rest that the date is lost among the mists of
time. Meanwhile, he proposes to toil vigorously
at the preparation of a new drama, that being the
only form of repose he indulges in; in other words,
he leaves off work and proceeds to carry bricks
uphill.
** * *
Edward Fordo, “the most remarkable
facial comedian of the age,” made his appearance
at Sydney Tivoli according to promise, and proved
as well up to description as could be reasonably
expected. That is to say he is a good comedian,
and, owing to his variety of expression, he can
pass’ as three or four comedians when necessary, and
if he could all appear on the stage simultaneously
he would fill a very long-felt want. Winton, the
ventriloquist, has fixed up some new and interest-
ing performances, and all the old members of the
co., including little Alma Grey, are doing well.
Also, the glad news is promulgated that comedian
Larry Dooley will be in evidence next Saturday.
The name of Dooley is probably intended as a
guarantee that the bearer is a comedian, it being
a tradition of the stage that all persons named
Dooley are more or less comic.

“M’lntyre’s Flirtation” put in a postponed
appearance at Melb. Princess’ last Friday. The
gloomiest anticipations were more than realised
by this ricketty dramatic offspring of “ Muldoon’s
Picnic.” However, as the poor little thine toddled

theatre for one night only, and is not likely to re-
peat its indiscretion at any future time, The
Bulletin begs to move that the circumstance be
expunged from the brilliant records of the Prin-
oess’. Carried unanimously.
** * *
“ The New Boy ” made a cheerful start on
Saturday. The extreme simplicity of its story
distinguishes “The New Boy” from many other
farces, ancient and modern. Massive Miss Jenny
Watt-Tanner has a husband able to pass fairly
well for her 14-year-old son, and circumstances
force this beardless adult into the garments of
youth and the position of a pupil at boarding-
school. There he gets torn asunder on the foot-
ball field, and inhumanly buckled by Bullock
major. Also, he is rather worried by the endear-
ments of a forward little minx, whose license to
roam around the academy and mash at random is
not more improbable than the discretion of a
married man in declining her invitations to hug.
There are no hiding places, or ordinary complica-
tions, attached to the scene of this imposture, and
when the new boy confesses his 30 years, and his
fine, large figure of a wife has publicly claimed
him for her own, nothing else needs to be
straightened up. A red-haired schemer and a
sudden rise in the shares of a dry champagne
company are extraneous incidents.
# # •
The cast of characters for “ The New Boy”
was the same at the Princess’ as when the joke
was first played in Sydney. All the newcomers—
Messrs. Bernage, Alderson, Mayeur, Cosham,
Roberts, and Miss Housley—look their parts
satisfactorily, besides being more or less convincing
in other respects. Perhaps Ernest Cosham, as
Bullock major, acts a few years in advance of his
Eton jacket, and probably Dion Boucicault would
make a better principal boy than Ralph Roberts,
but the objections in both cases are negative.
Miss Watt-Tanner is a toiver of strength, and
Miss Housley succeeds in seeming even girlier
and sweeter than sweet sixteen. Also, the new
comic edition of Clifton Alderson is a refreshing
change from his former self, whom one remembers
as the heroic leading gent, of the ill-fated Laura
Villiers expedition. Indeed, it is hard to recog-
nise our old friend, the Duke of Guiseberry, in
Felix Roach.

Evidently there has been an accident with the
tableau lady’s fleshings. The screen is accepted
as a hasty managerial device for concealing the
extent of the damage.

Charles Erin Verner, once an Australian
native comic singer, who has learned to act in
foreign parts, appeared at Melbourne Royal on
Saturday night. Mr. Verner is now an Irish
comedian of the best brand. He has a breezy
bhoyish style in humorous scenes, and much quiet
toree at serious moments. His own version of
ohainus O’Brien ” is a very inferior melodrama,
concluding with an impossible gallows and a
ridiculous reprieve for the prisoner, but Charles E.
Verner wins respect all the time. If he will
appear m an Irish play from somebody else’s pen,
and abandon his habit of singing more than is
good for the audience, the new arrival may do a
. r,s c business at the Royal. Apart from Verner’s
clever performance some very tolerable Milesian
treachery and patriotic valor are wasted upon
ohainus O'Brien.”
