AN APOLOGY.
This is NOT the Portrait of Miss MAY
GUMMAS.
This portrait was, in last week’s Bulletin,
erroneously printed as the portrait of the com-
plainant in the Sydney court-martial case.
Through an unfortunate mistake, which The
Bulletin greatly regrets, and for which it now
sincerely and unreservedly apologises, great annoy-
ance has been caused to Miss KATE CUM-
MINGS, a young and favorably-known Sydney
lady, by the publication of her portrait in the last
issue of this paper as that of “ MISS MAY
CUMMINS, complainant in the Sydney court-
martial case." The portrait was obtained in the
usual way, i.e., through an individual who devotes
himself to providing newspapers with photographs
for publication, and as the provider of that portrait
gave his assurance that he had himself seen the
lady at the court-martial, and as this alleged
portrait of “ Miss May Cummins” corresponded
with one previously published by another paper,
it was accepted and printed in good faith. The
Bulletin much regrets that it should have been
so deceived, and that pain should have been in-
flicted on Miss Kate Cummings, a lady in no
way connected with Miss May Cummins, or with
the court-martial proceedings before referred to.
The Wild-Cat Column .
When the Anglo-Australian Investment Finance
and Land Co., of Sydney, made its historic collapse, it
found itself curiously situated. The debenture-holders
(£350,000 worth of them) had the first call on the assets ;
the depositors had the second, and the shareholders the
third. As there weren’t enough assets to cover the
debentures, the depositors had no visible chance of get-
ting anything; as for the sharebo'ders, their case was
hopeless from the start. Then the depositors commenced
expensive law proceedings to upset the claims of the
debenture-holders, and. being defeated in the local courts,
they carried the case to the Privy Council, and the Privy
Council, after chewing it over for six or eight months,
has decided in favor of the debenture-holders, and ordered
the miserable depositors to pay a very robust bill of costs.
• • * ♦
The Union Bank of Australia has earned about
£44,700 during the past half-year, and pays a 6 per cent,
div., absorbing £45,000. The result is attributed partly
to the general falling off in profits, and partly to the
large suras written off to provide for bad and doubtful
debts. No one has ever suspected the Union’s solidity,
yet its £25 shares, which sold a few years ago at nearly
£BO, can now be had for £29.
♦ * * »
The old, old story. The Australian Deposit and
Mortgage Bank, which reconstructed some time ago in
the usual enthusiastic fashion, reports that “ it is impera-
tive that steps should be taken to reduce the rate of in-
terest payable to depositors and debenture-holders.”
The reason is that £730,000 of the bank’s advances .are at
present bearing no interest. And just here the same old
question naturally arises: If this £730,000 pays no interest
what chance is there of getting back the principal? A'd
if the principal, or a good part of it, should prove irre-
coverable, where will the depositors, &c., ultimately coma
in?
♦ ♦ ♦ *
In another big impending “stave-off,” The
Bulletin sorrowfully notes that one institution is “in”
£12,000 and another (a very careful concern) £35,000.
Neither has any security save bills of the stave-off con-
cern’s eus tomers. A man who can get discounting
facilities appears to have no need of actual capital in
some provinces. The trader who at first discounts only
his “ sweet ” paper, can in time get anything melted, and
the tons of accommodation stuff are never distinguished
until either a bolt is made or the inevitable meeting of
creditors comes around.
* * * *
In Sept, last a big liquor-house joyfully an-
nounced to one of its creditors who “ had his doots ” that
its (the b.l.h.'s) balance-sheet showed £50,000 surplus. In
Dee. the house whooped wildly to other creditors that
its surplus was £25,000. At a select meeting of the
creditors the other day it showed a surplus of but
£14,000 ! Provided all these statements were correct,
what a frightful illustration it is of the rapid depreciation
in value of trade assets ! The assets, by the way, consist
of stock, about £13,000 ; bills, £5000; cash, £600; and
book-debts, £45,000. Good old book-debts and bills!
Poor old cash ! Nearly as bad as a boom balance-sheet.
* » * *
N.S.W. Justice Manning has decided that a
man is insolvent on the day he cannot meet, out of his
own money, all the legitimate claims against him. How
many commercial men are, in these days of paper, solvent
in that sense? Also, Justice Manning is one of the
influential people who, a year or so ago, held that certain
N.S.W. hanks “ were perfectly solvent if only they were
given a little time.”

* * * *
A highly “ aristocratic ” firm of Melbourne
jewellers has been dropped on for a £3OOO fine
for defrauding the Customs (the Argus calls it
“evasion of duty”). The proprietor is very wealthy and
can afford to pay the fine 20 times over. Another big dis-
tributing jewellery house was dealt with only the other
day for smuggling dutiable jewellery in concertinas. Tub
Bulletin has already na< rated the swindle where 18-carat
gold chains were imported as brass-lamp pendants. There
are also three wholesale jewellery houses in Melbourne
and one in Adelaide which have made fortunes
at the game. Those which si ill continue operations
should cherish all "intelligent” employes, irespective of
their capacity, inasmuch as every expose of Customs eva-
sions so far has been made by' discharged employes. As
a matter of fact, the Customs cannot “follow” smuggled
jewellery unless “put up” to it. The very'compactness
of the article renders its smuggling easy, and no one but
an who knows the inner workings of his firm, can
afford sufficient evidence to the Customs to put the smug-
glers away.

The not altogether unexpected departure of a
rather big speculator has caused a bit of a shook in cer-
tain mercantile circles. The gentleman, now abroad,
left behind him a statement offering his creditors Is. 6d.
in the £, payable in six half-yearly instalments of 3d.,
and setting forth that he is not even a partner in the
prosperous firm of which he was by many regarded as the
principal; also, saying that if his creditors don’t believe
him he has a good mind to return at once and tell them
all about it. The amount involved is said to be £74,000.
Assets, £1126.

The rise in bank-shares and deposits, whsaih
started early in the present year, has been trjKrably
well maintained. Commercial of Australia preference
shares have risen from 19a. to 39a. Colonial preference,
which seemed to have vanished off the face of the earth,
have buyers at 245. National £lO preference are in
demand at the respectable figure of £6 2s. 6d., and the
long-lost ordinary shares of the same co. are again
saleable. Bank of Victoria £lO preference shares
have sold at £6 3s. A.J.S. shares (£7 paid up) have
recovered from 17s. 6d., or less, to 305., and the
City of Sydney £lO shares have sold at £4 3a. Royal
of Queensland, Union, N.S. W., and Australasia have
al'improved more or less. Meanwhile, B.N.Z. shares are
a vi offered at ss. without any sales reported ; Queensland
National (£4 10s. paid), which have also been for sale at
ss. for so long a period that the memory of man can
hardly recall a time when they were otherwise, remain as
before; and City of Melbourne, which have nererheen
even mentioned for months past, haven’t participated in
the rise, and continue an unknown quantity.

* * * *
Agent-General Playford, himself a market-
gardener, is making the London produce-depot boom.
The recent public sale in London of S. A. wines was a great
success. Circulars were distributed over the whole of
Great Britain, and £l3O was paid for a full-page advt. in
the Times. The whole of the wines in the catalogue were
sold at good prices. The S. A. Produce Dept, only acts as
agent for S. A. vignerons, but it guarantees the quality of
all wines sold.
* * * •
The latest piece of trade-assigneeship. A man
having become insolvent, a trustee was appointed. After
having the carcase long in his care he was called upon for
a statement. Result—estate panned out about £5O, owing
by a country institution ; trustee’s expenses collecting
same were nearly £46. When told to hand over the
balance he asked for time, a week or ten days. Granted.
Truly, the wind is tempered to the shearer.

A. J. Chappell, a South Africa cyanide man,
and moving spirit in a cyanide 00. (to be known as the
Austral Junction Co.), backed by several Broken Hillio-
naires, has secured all the likely-looking abandoned
shows on Pambula. About 100 acres have been taken up,
and the Golden Diorite Co.’s battery and leases have been
bought.
• ♦ * ♦
The self-feeders at Baker’s Creek are the beat
plant of the sort going in Australia. So impressed with
them is the adjoining (and never over friendly) Sunlight
Co., that it has bought the right to apply the principle to
the whole of the Sunlight battery. At Baker’s Creek,
self-feeding is said to give the battery a 30 per cent, in-
creased capacity as compared with hand-feeding.
The only battery on Bendigo running self-
feeders is the Virginia, and even there they are loosed
at askance. Wear-and-tear and frequency of stoppage
are asserted to neutralise their other advantages.
Among the N.S.W. batteries known to run
self-feeders are the Homeward Bound (Yalwal), the Went-
worth Goldfields Proprietary (Lucknow), and the Baker’s
Creek (Hillgrove).
• * • «
Further economies at Block 14 (8.H.). Man-
ager Simpson, who succeeded Zeb. Lane, in turn makes
way for the next in command. The manager of Block 14
is now only expected to be what in other mines is known
as an underground manager. The divided control falls
very largely on the metallurgist, who is now the most
important individual in the service of the mine. Zeb.
Lane used to be his own metallurgist.
The slimes from Block 10 concentrators are un-
usually rich— so rich that the co. has been able to dis-
pose of parcels to smelting go’s. It was the heavy loss in
the slimes which formed the principal basis of criticism of
Manager Warren’s process of" v concentration. The pro-
spect of a permanent market tor the slimes considerably
improves the outlook for the process.
Block 10 is calling for tenders for the purchase
of part, or the whole, of the big sulphide dump, which
contained, at the end of the last half-year, just under
40,000 tons. When silver was in the neighbourhood of 4s.
the dump could have been sold at 30s. per ton.

* * * *
First meeting of Londonderry shareholders a
howling success. Chairman North said the public took
over 400,000 shares, and letters of regret had been sent to
subscribers of over 11,000. It then developed into a love-
feast. Lou. Aarons, late of Sydney, said he had seen
the mine and subscribed 3000 shares—but whether he
mentioned that his brother had been appointed mining-
manager does not appear. Lord Fingall gave some assay-
ing figures all based on “if,” and one Gorrie said his
friend Shaw said he could get out three to five tons of
gold in eight to ten weeks. The Bulletin would like to
add its little opinion. The Londonderry is a phenomenal
surface-show—perhaps the richest ever seen, but whether
it will pay one div. or twenty on a capital of £700,0C0
no man knoweth. The chances are the shareholders
will never get their money back. The chairman also
announced that the vendors had acquired seven adjoir ing
leases, and would amalgamate them with the big mine
without any charge on the shareholders, and with visions
of “pups” the shareholders’ gratification was great.
* * ♦ *
Capitalists looking for mines and mostly
getting only leases—are daily arriving at Coolgardie.
They all come from, or are going to, London, where the
nuts are, and nine-tenths of them are sure to find mines
as long as the market holds out. The present game is to
throw cold water on the early floats and floaters. Some
excuse must be found, now that the batteries are going
up, and to hold the market these men and mines must be
passed out. One brilliant mining authority says: “I
believe London has of late been getting some of the best
Westralian properties just as at the beginning (hey got
all the worst.” Where and what are they ? London gets
no properties that are good at 'the price. It is next to
impossible to get a div. mine in W.A., because a shew
which would be worth £lO,OOO in Vic., where there is
plenty of water and cheap mining, would bring three
times the money in W.A., where there is neither—for the
boom is there.

• * * *
Mr. W. Merton, watchmaker, Sydney, late of
Hill End, in conjunction with Hr. Wiston Baker, Hill
End, has invented a new and simple self-opening tin, the
“Pull-wire.” A slight corrugation is made around the
top of the tin’s rim ; one end of a piece of thin steel-wire
is soldered in the corrugation, wound around the top of
the tin in the corrugation (the bed of the wire) to within
a Jin. of the other end, and is then passed through a
small hole in the top rim of the lid, and formed into a
loop. The lid is soldered on, and the hole through which
the wire passes hermetically sealed. To open the tin, you
hang the loop on a nail (or pass over anything, such
as a knife-handle, which will give you a grip) and pull
gently. The wire cuts the top of the lid almost off,
leaving only half-an-inch to act as a hinge ; but does not
leave any jagged edges whatever, and the whole contents
of the tin—whether meat, fish; jams or jellies—can be
emptied out in a solid form. The invention has been
syndicated in 2800 shares, and patented in 22 countries.
It is expected to prove a good thing.

INDICATE A DISORDERED LIVER.
CLEMENTS TONIC IS A
PROMPT AND POSITIVE CURE.
CLEMENTS TONIC DOES NOT
TREAT SYMPTOMS, IT
REMOVES THE CAUSE.
FOR PROOF READ THIS:—
Mr. Chas. Friar, Nelson, N.Z., who writes on
May 15tb, 1893 : —I am much pleased to bear testi-
mony to the value of Clements Tonic. For seven
years I suffered from chronic dyspepsia, and con-
sulted many doctors, and took gallons of different
medicines, none of which did me the slightest
good. Every meal I took used to cause me great
inward pain, and invariably I used to throw it up
again. At length I had to take all my meals per-
fectly dry, and in a recumbent position, but with-
out benefit. Accidentally I got hold of one of
Clements Tonic pamphlets, and after reading its
contents I resolved to try the medicine. After
taking the first bottle I could take my food with-
out lying down, and three bottles cured me en-
tirely. I can now take my food solid and liquid
together, without the slightest after pain or ten-
dency to throw it up. I feel perfectly well and
have gained considerable flesh, whereas when the
complaint was on me I was as thin as a skeleton.
My age is 42, and present weight eleven stone four
pounds. I shall consider it my duty to recom-
mend Clements Tonic to all who suffer as I have
done, —Chas, Friar, Nelson, N.Z.

Mrs. “Yellow Aster” Caffyn has started
a series of short stories called “ The Protegee of
Mrs. James Chisholm,” in a London journal.
Two Vic. Judges—Williams and Holroyd
—now have their sons as associates, having got
rid of other men to place their offspring in the
posts.
The father of the late levers, M.L.A., has
been elected for Melbourne City Council in his
son’s place. Probably a unique incident in civio
history.
February Review of Reviews sends round
the world a photo, of the Premiers in Conference,
with Nelson labelled Forrest and Forrest posing
as Nelson.
Miss C. H, Spence, of S.A., is about to
begin another crusade in favor of her system of
Effective Voting and the Representation of
Minorities.
Premier Forrest, of Westralia, who repre-
sents Bunbnry, where his family has lived for a
generation, is related to “ almost every one of bis
constituents.”
Journalist and pamphleteer John Norton,
“John English,” is understood to be studying for
the Bar. Hence his activity in rooting out
matters of legal and constitutional significance.
The next Vic. Supreme Court Judge will
come from these five: H. B. Higgins, I. A. Isaacs,
E. E. Mitchell, W. H. Irvine, and T. A’B.
Weigall. Most likely Higgins will be the man.
J. L. Parsons, ex-S.A. parson and Tory
politician, formerly N.T. Govt, resident, is the
author of a political novel shortly to be published
in London. Parsons’ politics are of a strong Ass.
flavor.
W. C. Wentworth, grandson of “the first
of first-born Australians,” has just been called to
the Bar from the Middle Temple, with honors in
Jurisprudence. He intends to practise in Sydney
both law and Rugby football.
Mount Morganaire D’Arcy’s pretty daugh-
ter, Lena, is now matrimonially allotted to a
dashing English guardsman (Lieut. Sidney Han-
key) and"her dot is fixed at the comparatively
small figure of <£looo a year.
An old and prosperous Hunter River
pioneer and cattle-breeder, John Nowlan, of
Eelah, died last week aged 80 years. He was an
erstwhile N.S.W. Parliamentarian, and for years
represented a Hunter constituency.
A hanker, lately released from a N.S. W.
gaol,*was, onhis way to his home, presented by a
thoughtful friend with a neatly-bound scrap-book
containing a full report of his trial and conviction,
with the after comments of town and country
papers.
Lindsay Cooper, night-overseer of S.A.
Register, recently retired from that paper after
56 years’service. Cooper came out to S.A. with
the first proprietor, Robert Thomas, and assisted
to bring out the first number in a tent at Hold-
fast Bay.
Ash, M.P. (S.A.) does not save all his
eloquence for Parliament. Recently he spoke for
three hours in Adelaide police court, in defence
of a man charged with pushing a truck on a foot-
path. Yet the magistrates only fined his client
10s.
Ferdinand von Mueller—who attributes
bis healthy old age principally to his going so
much barefooted—will, on retirement from the
Vie. Public Service at the end of the year, draw a
pension only a few pounds leas than the salary he
has been receiving.
“The player plays many parts.” Jacob
Garrard, of N.S.W., wharf-laborer, blacksmith,
engineer, book-keeper, land-agent, auctioneer,
M.L.A., Minister for Education, and Minister
for Industry and Labor, is but middle-aged yet
and is still going strong.
The proposed Land and Income tax has
produced quite a scare amongst the N.S.W. brigade
in London, and there will shortly be a large exodus
to Sydney (writes The Bulletin’s correspondent),
including Tanglefoot Tooth, Fitzwilliara Went-
worth, and W. C. Cooper.
J. W. Hackett, M.L.C., editor and part-
proprietor of West Australian, used to be a
thorn in the flesh of Dr. Madden (now Vic. Chief
Justice), with whom he closely contested Port
Melbourne on several occasions. Hackett is now
one of the strong men in W.A.’s political life.
Henry Frencham, of Richmond (Vic.),
who claims to have been the first discoverer of
gold at Bendigo in Nov., ’5l, revisited the old spot
last week. Since the first find -£70,000,000 worth
of gold has been taken from the field. Mr.
Frencham is 79 years of age and has 50 grand-
children.

Oscar Wilde is proceeding against the
“ Markis ” of Queensberry for criminal libel. Pro-
bably the nobleman has accused Oscar of some
conventionality. Mister Wilde would have been
satisfied to revenge himself with an epigram, were
he capable of evolving one quite as telling as
Queensberry’s uppercut.

Hercules Robinson, now spoken of as the
next Governor of the Cape, was accustomed in
Sydney to refer to his minute on the release of
bushranger Gardiner, sentenced to 32 years’ im-
prisonment, as his ablest performance.while N.S.
Wales Governor. The “ performance ” nearly
broke the back of Responsible Government.
Lord Brassey owns 26,000 acres of Wes-
tralian land, which, he says, he bought for
“purely philanthropic purposes,” intending to
establish a pauper colony in Westralia with
“ General ” Booth. The philanthropic idea did
not come off, and Brassey’s land is now worth
double its cost price. Virtue is its own reward.
The opulent Capt. “Matt.” Byrnes,
drowned last week at Pyrmont wharf, Sydney,
always had the honorary privilege of bringing up
the*Beautiful Harbor in one of his small fleet of
ferry-boats the prelates of the R.C. Church who
arrived by mail steamers. He so piloted Arch-
bishop Vaughan on his arrival, and, later on,
Card. Moran and Bishop Higgins.
Ib is related of the late Detective Elliott,
the best-known practitioner of his day in N.S.W,,
that he abhorred divorce oases and never volun-
tarily engaged in them. Once an old client, whom
he could not refuse, employed him to “shepherd’
his wife. Elliott accepted the commission, but he
waited upon and warned and advised the lady,
and in the end the couple lived happily.
A prince who lately visited Australia was,
at his own request, shown over the works of a
certain private 00., and recognised the intelligence
and civility of the senior workman, who acted as
guide in the absence of the manager, by present-
ing him with—a threepenny piece. The coin has
since been put in a suitable plush frame, and
adorns a conspicuous place on the office wall.

the wife of Mr. Ninian Melville, of Sydney, had
presented that gentleman with triplets, everyone
assumed that the victim was Ninian Melville, ex-
Chainnan of Committees of N.S.W. Assembly,
who lately lost his billet. “ And Satan finds
some mischief still for idle hands to do,’’remarked
the populace. But it wasn’t that Ninian Melville,
Mr. Freeman Thomas, Brassey’s son-in-
law, who is coming out to Melbourne as an
A.D.0., is, like his father-in-law, the son of a
wealthy contractor. Freeman Thomas, pere, was
a publican on a mammoth scale. He catered for
the Covent Garden Promenade Concerts, kept
hotels, and made money “ hand over fist ” in Lon-
don. His connection with Brassey is another link
between the beerage and the peerage.

who was a great expert in wine and precious

with you Irish ; you can’t give a straightforward
answer to a direct question.” “ Phaw,” says the

marry yon or ye’d know the difference.”

Re Chief-Justice Madden’s alleged remarks
about the poorness of the Vic. bar, “What about
the Supreme Court bench ? ” asks a BULLETIN
correspondent. “ Holroyd is the only first-class
man there, though Hood is good. Hodges looks
very sharp and isn’t; A’Beckett always reserves
his decision, and is apparently not equal to taking
reliable notes ; Williams would be a good man if
he could, but is lazy : while Madden is still an
advocate and very weak in his digest of facts;
The Supreme Court bench does not exhibit the
power of putting aside its class prejudices, but
somehow always appears to view the working
classes with a stern aspect compared to the suavity
exhibited towards the highly respectable and
moneyed. As for future Judges, Higgins, Topp
(if he abjured certain little eccentricities), and
Isaacs would be equal to any of the present bench
except Holroyd.”
The Rev. Dr. Momerie, the heretical
theological professor from London, who has been
globe-trotting through amused those
who met him by his dandified ways. He wears
dainty black silk socks, with white embroidered
clocks, and patent-leather shoes at least two sizes
too small for him ; clothes of the most elegant
cut, and a moustache trimmed as carefully as that
of the veriest dude. The philosophical conversa-
tion which some of those who met him
expected was not forthcoming. He jabbered only
the flimsiest small talk —cheap nothings—in the
simpering manner of a debutante in her first
season. He is so careful of his precious self
that he wears a muffler and a gresit-coat on even
the hottest days.
During the good old “stone-walling”
period of N.S.W. Parliamentary history, while
an attempt was being made by the virtuous Oppo-
sition of the day to delay a design of its enemy,
the corrupt and despicable Government, poor
Harold Stephen, in a stone-walling speech, was
called to order for describing someone as a “devil.”
Harold proceeded coolly to argue the matter on a
point of order, and went into the whole history of
the Devil, his private and public character, his
origin and probable final end, his habits, appear-
ance and general manners, his rise, decline and
fall, his influence, moral, political and ecclesias-
tical, the legends concerning him ; also the popu-
lar misconceptions about him. He vindicated him
in an elaborate disquisition, and then proved con-
clusively that there never was such a thing as a
Devil, nor did he ever possess the attributes alleged
concerning him ; but in case he ever had existed,
the spotlessness and blamelessness of his character
were clearly shown. After rambling on with
immense erudition, touching lightly for an hour or
so by the way on the Faust version, both Goethe’s
and Marlowe’s, Harold Stephen sat down, and
was immediately ruled out of order.
The late Capt. Darby, of the Orient liner
Ormuz, who, to the great regret of many afloat
and ashore, lately died at sea, aged 38 years, was
a man who lived for his ship ; he had no sports, no
hobby ; the ship’s welfare was the be-all and end-
all of his existence. Like Captain Tuke, of the
Austral, he was an old “Worcester” boy. This
training-ship Worcester is still lying in the
Thames, and still trains most of the midshipmen
for the English mercantile service; Oapt. Darby
was a 13-year-old boy when he joined her. He
first went to sea in ’BO, in the Lord Warden, one
of the old line of Blackwall sailing ships, which
began running to Melbourne in ’4B, so that from
the commencement of his career Captain Darby
was identified with the Australian trade. In ’BO
he became chief officer of the Renown, a ship
of the same service, and in ’Bl joined tin*
Lusitania, of the Orient line, taking the post of
fourth officer, the highest position assigned by
this company to officers coming frqm another
service. He rose to be second officer of the Cuzco,
then chief-officer of the Austral, and in ’9O com-
mander of the Chimborazo. His first trip as
commander was on a yachting cruise to Norway.
He was given command of the Orient in ’9l, but
in ’92 he had SO bad an attack of influenza that he
was prostrated awhile, and when well again took
the Chimborazo for another yachting trip. Re-
turning, he was appointed commander of the
Ormuz, and died on his second voyage from the
effects of a fresh attack of influenza. His wife
died 12 months ago last January, after a short
four years of wedded life.

Marital infidelity, murder and misdirected
suspicion, are fictional “properties ” so well worn
as to require rather more delicate handling than
they receive in “ Mortgaged Years,” the work —
under the pseudonym of “R. K. Dee of Mr.
O’Hara Wood, of the Victorian Bar. The book,
in fact, is less a performance than a promise, for,
though its general lines of construction are not
much less conventional than its materials,
there is an ingenuity about some of the in-
cidental developments which should make the
author heard of again to greater advantage.
Extremely well-conceived, for instance, is the
device by which the self-suspected Marston
is made to enlighten about hit enlightenment the
frisky dame whose negleet of the Seventh Com-
mandment is the root of all the evil. The situa-
tions, also, are, upon the whole, stronger than the
style, which is, in spots, not very dimly suggestive
of the London Journal. This fault, however, will
probably disappear with practice, and may fairly
have set against it certain touches of imagination
and pathos which take the book well out of tin
category of the commonplace.
A needy and benighted man,
Of woe-bent form and visage wan,
Attracted by a trembling ray
Which seemed at times to die away,
And sad as suffering wretch who sighs
Because none share his miseries,
Wandered across the gloomy wold
To find a shelter from the cold.

The advisableness of appointing future
stipendiary magistrates from the Bar is being
considered by the Reid Government. In England
and Scotland stipendiaries must be barristers.
Frugal S. A. ! The only new appointment
under the Factories Act is that of Mrs. Zadow,
who has done much good work among the poor of
Adelaide at her own expense, to be inspector at
.£65 per annum.

The utter impossibility of
telling how a new Act will
work is shown by a Maori-
lind return, which states that,
in the 23 years of provincial
Parliaments (1853-76), 2323
Acts were passed, of which all
bub 105 had to be repealed.
Under the present Parlia-
mentary system (1876-94), 2972
Aobs have been put on the
statute-book, and already 1602
have been repealed. Yet the
first idea of the average poli-
tician is that it is the chief
duty of Parliament to make
laws, more laws, keep on
making laws, though, more
often than not, the law is un-
necessary-even harmful; and
it generally is far easier to
pass a bad law than to repeal
it.

row over the recent absence

once he went away on a long

son to run his Assembly elec-

right, until some fool of a
voter arose and asked “old
Jack ” whether Mr. Want was
a Freetrader or a Protectionist.
“ How the devil should I
know I” snuffled the ancient—
“why, the blanky man’s
away !”

In the Morris v. Syme case plaintiff stated
that he did not consider it incongruous for a man
not holding a vote in an electorate to endeavor to
represent that electorate in Parliament. Why
should it be so, seeing that though a conviction for
crime deprives a voter of his franchise, it doesn’t
debar him from standing for a constituency ?
The Board of Health at York (W.A.) has
issued a notice that the local water-supply “is
quite unfit for use unless boiled.” It might have
gone a little further, and stated that all Westralia
—including its Government—is unfit for use un-
less boiled. The Government probably wants to
be boiled more than any other part of the concern.
Was the incitement to murder, alleged
against anarchist Andrews, equally widespread,
grave, or persuasive with the incitement to
assassinate the late Czar, promulgated by poet
Swinburne? Yet English law overlooked the
latter, and the House of Commons declined to
interfere, whereas, in Sydney, law and order tried
and sentenced Andrews.

Melb. Board of Works lately sent to Eng-
land a large order for cement —which could have
been supplied locally at an equally cheap rate.
The duty came to .£20,000, and the Aegus heads
its article —in which it points to the transaction
as a horrible example of the evils of Protection—
with the words, “.£20,000 thrown away.” The
Aegus evidently thinks that the money received
for Customs duties is put in an iron box and cast
into the deep, damp sea. The fact that the
.£20,000 was paid by the people to the Govern-
ment, and was used by the Government for the
benefit of the people, and that, if it hadn’t been
paid by the people in this way, it would have had
to be paid in some other way, will probably be
quite new and surprising information to the
Aegus.
“Sweet Marie,” the song which has
become so popular all through the colonies, has
just been issued by Messrs. W. H. Glen & Co., of
Melbourne, in No. 28 of their well-known musical
annual, entitled the Colonial Musical Cabinet,
which is certainly a marvellous shilling’s-worth of
music, as, in addition to “Sweet Marie,” the
book contains no fewer than 14 other popular
songs, complete with words and music, including
“ Because I Love Thee So,’’ “ She Wore a Wreath
of Roses,” “Mottoes Framed Upon the Wall,”
“ Simon the Cellarer,” “Blind Girl to Her Harp,”
and “ Where’er You Walk.”
Frank Harley Davis developed his early
symptoms of brain and muscle at Toowoomba (Q.),
where his father has long-time presided over the
Highfields State school. At 10 years of age the
boy asserted his independence by joining the
shearing camp at Jondaryan Station, on the
Darling Downs, as “ picker-up ”of fleeces. Later,
he became a pupil-teacher under his father, but,
finding the scholastic atmosphere uncongenial, he
elected to become a professional shearer at the age
of 19. Five years later he was known through-
out Australia as one of the fastest wool-gatherers
on record —the “ringer’’ of the majority of the
sheds of the Barcoo, Diamantiria, Culf, and W.Q.
squattages. On one test occasion, at Lome, he
shore 400 full-fleeced sheep in two days of Bhrs.
20mins. each. At Isis Downs he shore 200 sheep
in hours; and, during the season of ’B3, he
shore 24,500 sheep, an almost incredible
record, seeing that the average shearer’s tally
is 90 per day! Owing to failing health
Davis dropped shearing after a while and
accepted service with John Falconer, a mining
expert and engineer, then employed by the old
railway department. The boss encouraged his
young employe’s natural taste for mechanics. It
was then that Frank Davis conceived the idea of
the Calyx drill, which, after long experiment,
was perfected in ’93. While exhibiting his drill
at Brisbane Exhibition in ’9O, the subject of this
notice saw the Wolseley sheep-shearing machine
at work, operated with it on a single sheep, and
thought he could go one better. Three years
later, in pursuance of this notion, he shore through
twosheds with the Wolseley,and at the second shed
made his improved implement at the station smithy
out of old cross-cut saws and other waste metal.
Experts now freely admit that the Davis sheep-
shearing machine (which will be driven by elec-
tricity) takes a tremendous stride in advance of
all previous inventions of the kind. Both patents
will be introduced to the industrial world by their
inventor. “At a modest computation,’’Davis ex-
pects to net .£200,000 from the Calyx drill within
14 years (the Vic. Government has already paid
.£2500 for the privilege of using five drills), and
the sheep-shearing machine is expected to earn a
big revenue for the proprietary company now
being formed in Melbourne for its manufacture
and sale. Frank Davis is an ardent Democrat,
and possesses the natural force and magnetism
which go to make leaders of men. In the old
days he exercised great influence among the
Queensland shearers, and might easily have dam-
aged his future by becoming a member of Parlia-
ment. The late Francis Adams met Davis in the
banana country, and a great friendship sprang up
between them. From the brainy “ ringer ” Adams
gained much of that insight into the toil and
hardships of the workers of the West, which he
frequently turned to account in his wiitings.

The first murder of a white man since the
Tongans were converted to Christianity 50 years
ago, happened on last New Year’s eve, says The
Bulletin’s correspondent, when a young car-
penter, Low, a Maorilander, who had only been
about a year living at Lifuka, Haabai, was killed
by a native, Siale Vani, a grandson of Pita Vi.
Pita *Vi was one of the first converts to Christi-
anity, and the first Wesleyan native ordained
minister in the Tongan Group. For years he was
chaplain to old King George Tubou I.

The curse going “ home.” Hindoo boot-
makers, at per day, now compete with the
Briton.
New Japanese discovery of “ Taka-koj ”
will knock out the distilleries. It is a new fer-
ment. Put a bag of sugar into a cask of water
with a little bran and taka-koj, and it ferments
until it is just half as strong as whisky. Put a
tap in and fill your jug ! This is a bigger thing
than smashing China up, if true.
A cow calved while being taken over the
North Shore (Sydney) ferry, the other day. The
man in charge of the cow was fearfully embar-
rassed, but the man in charge of the boat rose
calmly to the height of the occasion. He waited
till the interesting event was consummated, and
then demanded “ Another penny, please—for the
calf!”

The Hopetouns saw the big match from
the grand-stand. On Monday, when the first
English innings closed at 385, the Governor, in
manifest perturbation, changed his quarters, and
tried to extort what consolation he might from
Stoddart and Iredale. On the final day, when it
was all up with Australia, the Countess drove
home to substitute a triumphal white robe for her
sombre-colored gown, bringing back with her
“Lord Hope, if you please,” who was allowed his
only glimpse of the match—presumably in honor
of the discomfiture of “the colonials.”
* i *
An interested spectator of the big match
was lively, blue-eyed Mrs. Giffen, her chagrin
whenever hubby failed to “come off” showing
itself only in extra hilariousness. During the last
two hours of the contest, her laugh was more
frequent than ever. But, according to the wife of
another wicket celebrity, who viewed the down-
fall of her country with a complacency perhaps ex-
cusable under the circumstances, the smiles of the
captain’s better-half were, at this stage, pathetic
attempts indeed. The “grand old cricketer”
himself piloted cups of tea and similar marks of
devotion through the crowd until, despite his
hideous blazer, every married woman within eye-
shot voted him a “pet of a husband.”
• # •
The London influenza epidemic has caused
a tremendous rise in the price of eucalyptus oil.
What sort of eucalyptus oil, The 15ulletin
wonders, do they retail in London ? A great pro-
portion of the draught eucalyptus oil ordinarily
sold by the average Australian chenr'st is fraudu-
lent rubbish, consisting of, say, a-Hfth of eucalyp-
tus extract, a-fifth of olive oil, and three-fifths of
spirits of turpentine. “The public will have
cheap oil, and we give it to them.” Not only is
such draught oil an imposture—which, by the way,
would, under an honest legal-commercial system,
be punished with gaol —but some of the bottled
oils, being made from the wrong sort of eucalyp-
tus, are of very small aceount indeed.
« * «
The tenth anniversary of the departure of
the N.S.W. Brigands for the Soudan was cele-
brated in Sj T dney on 3rd inst. The ranks of the
misguided swashbucklers have not yet begun to
appreciably thin, but it may be safely predicted—
The Bulletin takes pleasure in predicting it regu-
larly every year—that the last survivor of the ex-
pedition will be the donkey that they brought
back with them. That donkey will eat his Soudan
dinner alone after all the rest have fled to Heaven.
It is the fittest that survives.
• * •
Commandant Hutton is in such a frantic
hurry to purge the N.S.W. army, that he cannot
see the necessity for equal justice. An ex-surgeon
swears that a certain captain was suffering from
an aggravated form of a certain disease, and was
treated for it while he continued to do duty and
draw his pay. Now, if a gunner had had the
same disease, he would have been kept in the
hospital, forcibly if necessary, till cured, and
would have forfeited his pay for the time he was
there. Why this difference ?
* * #
They now call it “ the Victorian knavey.”
Miss Ada Delroy is making her
second visit to Australia under engage-
ment to the veteran variety campaigner,
Mr. Harry Rickards. After an absence
of five years (spent in India, Great
Britain, and other lands) she has come
back younger and sprightlier than ever.
There is a refined sauciness about Ada
Delroy, and what she does—song and
dance, serpentine whirl, or comedy busi-
ness with her brother, James Bell—is
done prettily. A sense of humor is
unusual among little ladies who look
sweet, and The Bux.letin publishes Miss
Delroy’s merry face for the particular
reason that she can raise a laugh as
daintily as she lifts her nimble leg. In
short, this clever variety artist has more
understandings than two.

A temperance item. Melbourne
policemen hate Sunday duty—rounding
up the publicans. Every “ peeler ”
aspires to be a publican, and the fellow-
feeling makes him wondrous kind. Two
officers had the delectable job of removing
a very dead body from a suburban house
recently. Coming out for a breath of
fresh air, “Phew! Mac.,” said Mick,
“ it’s worse than foxin’ the blessed
publicans. ”
Inspector - General Fosbery, before the
matter came into court, instructed one of his detec-
tives to investigate the-private character of the
prosecutrix in the Sydney military court-martial
case. If the procedure be correct in this case,
why is not a State detective laid on to investigate
the lives of all girls bringing similar charges,
before the court deals with the accusations?
# ♦ #
Quite lately Mrs. Ellis Rowan, the artist,
bad her plate, pictures, and other valuables stolen.
Ere long the thief was caught, and the lady sent
for to identify some trifle of hers still in his
possession. When she arrived she heard a pitiful
tale —a consumptive wife, the man said, having
been his incentive to dishonesty. Mrs. Rowan
went to an address given her by accused, and, sure
enough, found there a bed-ridden invalid.
Mercifully refusing to lay a charge against the
husband, she bargained only that he should point
out to her the different pawnshops where her pro-
perty was deposited. This was soon done, though,
before the owner had redeemed half her belong-
ings, her purse was empty, and she had to visit
the bank.
* * *
Dear Bulletin, —Every morning a cluster
of poorly-clad persons may be seen waiting in
Macquarie-st., Sydney, near the District Court.
A notice grimly blazoned on the wall reads : “In-
digent persons desirous of gaining admission to the
metropolitan hospitals at the cost of the vote for the
maintenance of sick paupers should apply at this
office at 10.30a.m. daily.” The unfortunates have
to air themselves for hours with this grim chronicle
relating their trouble. Over and over again I have
seen some miserably sick man, with his meagre
bundle of possessions at hand, huddled on th%
pavement, and trying to slumber away the tedium
of waiting for the medical mandate. To every
passer such men appeared only as indigent persona.
What a brutal exposition of misfortune it all is !
Thorold D.
* • •
Complaints are made of the starvation
wages paid for work got through the instrumen-
tality of Melb. Charity Organisation Society.
The officials reply that they “ don’t interfere
between employer and applicantthey leave the
question of pay to be settled between the grizzly
bear and the rabbit. The society, being supported
by fat men, is naturally run in their interests. It
is not a charity society so much as a combination
of a private detective .office and a bureau for the
supply of cheap labor to subscribing employers.
* • *
Dear Bulletin, — lie a much-discussed
subject. I think the present poverty-stricken
condition of the legal profession is due in great
measure to overcrowding, the result of solicitors’
blind meanness in giving clerks articles gratis—
not with the laudable intention of breaking down
the barriers of a so-called privileged profession,
but in order to get cheap labor. Clerks thus
articled only get 10s. or 15s. a week. Such mean-
ness results in said clerks, when admitted to
practice, entering into competition with their
former masters and carrying away a portion of
their work. Another cause is that numerous
clerks, formerly in the employ of solicitors, despite
the stringent provisions of the Attorneys Act (con-
stituting the legal trades-union), carry on the
practice of conveyancing and other legal work
whether capable or not, and a section of the public,
rather than incur the ordinary fees of a solicitor,
risks getting bad titles by employing such unquali-
fied persons.—Yours, &c., Veritas.
# * #
The woman here, writes a BuLLETiN-man
from Maoriland, is a wonder. To see her get
about on bicycles—in skirts—would make you
shudder. The reform-dressed female is an atro-
city. Why doesn’t she shorten her skirts ?
Knickerbockers are too absurd. I should like
Hopkins to be here a few days—he would look
more solemn than ever, and the pathos of his eyes
would increase; but he would make Australia
roll over with laughing. The women generally
are fine creatures, some of them extremely tall
and graceful. I went to one political meeting,
attended by a large number of women. It was a
prohibitionist-speaker. They didn’t appear to be
interested, and acted as they do in church—stared
round and criticised each other’s toilettes. When
a round of applause came they looked round
hastily, with a “ What-was-that-he-said-I-
wasn’t-listening ” expression. There were seve-
ral benevolent old ladies there, taking a motherly
interest in the function and the speaker, who was
fairly young ; but they seemingly took little heed
of the subjects under discussion. Not one lady
applauded and, if they had any sentiments, they
Miss ADA DELROY, of the Rickards Variety Go
kept them dark. I could see several gushing
creatures giving glances of undisguised admira-
tion at the orator, and I required little imagina-
tion to picture the time when the successful
politician in Maoriland will be young, handsome,
nd unmarried, and will be the recipient of worked
Uppers and braces at Xmas-time. The “ mother-
and-marriageable-daughters ” vote will be assidu-
ously catered for by young ambitious men.
• # #
A Melb. lady typist rides to town every
day all the way from Camberwell on her bike.
Her travelling attire, as a rule, consists of blouse
and divided skirt. The latter looks little more
convenient than the ordinary style of petticoat for
pedalling, and far less becoming, especially from
a rear-view obtained when its wearer dismounts
on encountering a herd of dairy cows. And cows
are common in Camberwell.

The Australian Mahatmosophists what
remains of them after the Judge split—are to have
a convention in Sydney about Easter. The real
live Countess of the society, who admittedly
“played her title for all it was worth” in America,
Countess Wachtmeister, is coming from India to
spend a few months in the “kawlinies,” and will
arrive in time to be present. She is one of the
dupes, not the knaves, of the brother-and-sister-
hood, and her purse has bled freely for “ the
cause ” of Koot Hoomi and Co.

Dear Bulletin, —That separate pavilion-
entrance for the English professional cricketers
at Melbourne was an indefensible snobbery. Not
so the separate hotel-accommodation so much
commented on, because less “ frill ” and more
ease was what the p:os. wanted—and got—by an
arrangement which relieved them of unaccustomed
social restraints. It is always the conscious
inferior who is most jealous of line-drawing ; the
man who knows that he is below the salt by a
mere conventionality shrinks from doing himself
an injustice by placing his superficial inferiority
in evidence above it. —A.
# # *
The English Saturday Review gives over
a column-and-a-half of its very exclusive space to
a notice of H. A. White’s remarks on Ned Kelly,
by v hose exploits it is much impressed. Kelly is
known to Australians as an ugly, commonplace,
cold-blooded, utterly unehivalrous ruffian who
cost the community a lot of blood and money
before he was caught and hanged ; but the Satur-
day sees in him a noble outlaw, a modernised
Robin Hood, and remarks that the story of his
l'°e is an episode “in an imperfectly-evolved
~society, in which much of the virtue and more of
the skill is on the side of the rebels.” Delicious !
The writer of the article plainly regrets that such a
hero of romance was not better appreciated by his
local contemporaries. Couldn’t the police collect a
shipment of a dozen Kellys for export to England
—just to oblige the gentleman ?

• • #
Australian military officers in Victoria, it
appears, have the joint privilege of nominating one
youth a year for a commission in the Imperial
army. No undue noise has been made about this
fact, and Major Tulloch, the recently-departed
Victorian swashbuckler, although only a British
visitor, has secured the “ honor ” for his son in the
absence of any application from a properly-con-
stituted “colonist.” Now the properly-consti-
tuted are howling because knowledge of the con-
cession was not imparted to them. But it doesn’t
really matter. Australia doesn’t want to hire her
sons out as nigger-stalkers in the Imperial in-
terests, but the fact shows how ready the im-
ported hero with the padded chest is to grab with
both hands.
* # #
When Joseph Battenberg was at the
Jenolan caves he is reported to have “made a few
remarks” in eulogy of Mr, Jeremiah Wilson for
his energy in exploring these natural wonders.
The Bulletin feels sorry for J. Batt., as well as
for princes in general. The average prince is a
dreadfully bad speaker, and when he is tired
with scrambling through oaves and things he
feels grumpy and disinclined for oratory, just like
any other man. Vet he is constantly running up
against heavy social obligations, which call on him
to make a few well-chosen remarks about Mr,
Jeremiah Wilson, or Mr. William Smith, or some
other person who doesn’t in the least degree in-
terest him. Even the smallest kind of prince is
expected to be a flowing river of well-chosen re-
marks, and the burden lies heavy on his soul.
The only relief he gets is that he occasionally goes
to a strange country, and there he can use up all
his old remarks over again, and no one knows that
they are considerably the worse for wear.

Cosmos for February has some in-
teresting reminiscences of Butler Cole
Aspinall, the Vic. advocate and wit, by
Mr. Chas. Bright; and to the publisher
The Bulletin is indebted for permission
to reproduce the 27-year-old photograph
of Aspinall and Dailey which appears
in this issue. When Aspinall came to
Melbourne 40 years ago, under engage-
ment to take charge of the Argus Parlia-
mentary staff, Vic. Parliament boasted
but a single Chamber, which sat in St.
Patrick’s Hall, Bourke-street, from three
till six on four afternoons a week. The
“staff” comprehended only Bright and
another youth named Mackenzie, and
Aspinall’s comment was that if one of
them took ill the strength of his army
would be seriously impaired. In the
same number of Cosmos Mrs. Charles
Bright interviews Mrs. Brough, Mr. G.
B. Barton writes on Privy Council ap-
peals, and Col. Bell collects some useful
information concerning the Nicaragua
canal—making the issue the most read-
able yet published.
* • •
In Otago (M.L.) there is a prohibi-
tion district called Olutha. The other
day, when the train stopped at Waihola,
outside the prohibited district, the usual
rush was made on the refreshment-room
by the passengers. Among them was a
well-known and highly-respected citizen
who_ sidled up to the bar and asked unob-
trusively for a simple “ginger-wine.” He
drank it at a gulp and made for the door,
where he acted as if he had swallowed a
dose of kerosene. Then he returned to the
bar, and began firing off violent abuse at
the barman, who, he asserted, had tried
to poison him. “You asked for ginger-
wine and you got it,” interjected the
barman. “I asked for ginger-wine!”
rejoined the poisoned man, “Great Scott,
don’t you know what ginger-wine is?
They know considerably better than that
up Clutha way.” Then the barman
saw light, and he smiled gently and
soothingly, and, placing a bottle of Galley
whisky before the man from the prohibition dis-
trict, he told him to help himself. Then all was
peace. Which exactly fits in with N.S.W.
Speaker Abbott’s experiences during his recent
visit to Maoriland. “ Total prohibition,” says the
Speaker, “ seems a failure as it was in prohibi-
tionist Mildura in 1892. I never saw so much
drunkenness as at the latter place. When the
Maoriland women got the vote they made a great
descent on the sale of liquor—hence all this sly
drinking.”
“ Seeing he would not last many minutes
and wanted to die quietly, I told the family there
was no likelihood of a change till next day, and
induced all to retire. He fell into his last sleep,
and in five minutes his heart stopped. I turned
into my bed in the same room and soundly slept
till seven next morning, for it had not been pos-
sible to obtain sound slumber whilst attending
this case, and it was a fortnight since I came.
Rising, the family were informed, and my engage-
ment ended. Cruel? No, He wanted to die
quietly in sleep, and people fussing about only
annoyed him. What was done was the best for
ft lh” —A Sydney Nurse's narrative.
* m »
A new sulphurous evangelist is raging
in Maoriland. One of his recent converts
was an Auckland negro, who was so badly
scared by the missionary that he went
straight away and drowned himself in another
man’s tank of drinking-water to escape from his
horrible thoughts of the hereafter. Every evan-
gelist of any account leaves a trail of lunatics and
suicides in his devastated wake, and it is about
time the law compelled the itinerant preacher to
fit up beside his church a room with all the latest
appliances for painless extinction, so that his con-
verts may suicide decently and in order. Even in
the cause of religion no man has a right to drive
another man into anybody’s supply of drinking-
water.

founded “ at ’Ome ” find “ colonial ” soil the most
fertile, once the seed is planted. The Catholic-
Apostolic Church (London) was founded many
years ago by 12 young gentlemen who were ap-
pointed “ Apostles.” Of these, 11 are dead, and
the existence of the survivor appears to be an
essential factor in the existence of the creed.

months ago, and has been sent out here to recover

“ IMITATION
is the
SINCEFEST FLATTERY.”
The articles most imitated are
CLEMENTS TONIC and
FLETCHER’S PILLS.
WHY?
BECAUSE they have the best reputation and
are most in demand by the public, consequently,
every dishonest trader tries to trade on their fame
and renown.
It is really wonderful that the Australian people
are so slow to see the truth as regards the unscru-
pulous quacks and charlatans who prey on the
credulity of the Australian public. Scarcely a
week ever passes but what the press exposes the
barefaced and shameful practices of some of these
harpies, yet, in face of all, they seem to thrive,
and the paper which hounds them down most thrives
fattest on their advertisements and cracks cham-
pagne over each balance-sheet. Clements Tonic
and Fletcher’s Pills have never yet stooped to
deception and misrepresentation. They are scien-
tific therapeutical agents, and evidence as to their
value can be found in every district, and, such
being the case, it is unnecessary to further extol
their virtues.

glisten !—•
See ample cause for sticking to her seat.

Are by nature pure and good ; and his Honor,
Justice Hood,
Truly says that in the hearing of divorces
They know what all the hearers may expect,
So they come, although the duty be unpleasant.
To the gallery and cough when the giddy
lawyers scoff—
A reminder that the ladies who are present
Wish the matter to be treated with respect.
Once a petticoated visitor felt lonely
If his Honor chose to say—“ Bid that woman go
away ! ”
But we soon may see the notice, “ Ladies Only,”
Inviting all the darlings to be there.
Then the narrative of love that was unlawful
Will be chastened, in a sense, by the modest
audi-enoe,
And, should the full particulars sound awful,

Apropos the extra sentences lately passed
on criminals who had come to N.S.W. from Vic-
toria, an old Melb. detective lately told the writer
that there were many Sydney-made thieves in
Melbourne, the deterrent Influx of Criminals
Act notwithstanding. Why? A once-residenb
of Victoria, though convicted in N.S.W., can-
not be expelled the Southern province, nor can he
be imprisoned under the Act alluded to.

As already mentioned, “Black Maria”
has, in the interests of retrenchment, been abol-
ished in the Melb. suburbs, and the prisoners have
now to walk from the lock-ups to the gaol. At a
lock-up the other day two prisoners refused to
walk to gaol, but the sergeant kept them without
food, and they gave in.

At Walla Walla, down Albury way, a
novelty was lately introduced into a marriage
festivity. The bride’s father, a German, went
round the company after dinner and took up a
collection for the local hospital. The collection
came to <£3 15s. 6d., which at £ for £ subsidy
makes the hospital gainer by £7 11s.

At Maclean a woman and her two married
daughters have each given birth to twins in one
week. “Why don’t the men propose?” promises
to be a burning question at Maclean.
• * *
A remarkable case was recently tried at
Roma (Q.). One black had killed another with a
nulla. The crime arose out of aboriginal super-
stition. It was alleged that the deceased, a medi-
cine-man, had procured a human bone, blackened
it, smeared it with human blood, and pointed it
with maleficent intent at the prisoner and his gin
while they slept. Consequently the gin fell sick,
and the prisoner, having ascertained the cause,
brained ..the wizard with a nulla. Griffith, 0.J.,
took a lenient view of the case and inflicted three
years’ penal servitude on the murderer. It is
asserted, by the way, that this bone superstition
extends right throughout Australia.

* • *
A nigger executed for murder the other
day in Tonga made an edifying end. He stated
in his dying speech that he loved his king and
country, also the minister and the inspector of
police. Few men who are going to be hanged
really love the inspector of police, but the state-
ment was probably mere pious hysteria. Then he
asked that his Bible should be hanged along with
Inm, and, the request being granted, the nigger
and his Scripture were jerked off the tree
together.

O the names, the names, the honeyed names they carry,

Awful, ghastly, sinful names on which lo marry—•
Names enough to strike an undertaker dead 1
Here s a rift within a Lovey Lutey, mark you 1

Parson W. G.
Spence propounds
and maintains
that, under any
circumstances,
“the truest and
wisest legislation
is that which
makes the path of
the wrong - doer
difficult and trou-
blous, and the
path of the right-
doer easy and
tr i urn ph an t. ” Nob
so, ber-loved ber-
retheren ! For, if
it be made easy
and triumphant,
if it be made pro-
fitable and re-
wardable, to do
right, what credit
can there be in
doing right? Do
we canonise an icicle for nob
burning with the flames of lust?
Dowe count the millionaire virtuous be-
cause he does nob go “barbering” hotels?
Should a dried-up curmudgeon be extolled for his
temperance because even a spoonful of generous
liquor “puts a head on him?” No, ber-loved
ber-retheren ! Let us make right-doing difficult,
and then we who overcome the enemy shall be
worthy of the nimbus. Otherwise, virtue becomes
a profession and a means of livelihood. Hard
battling is the test of quality.

The many friends of Mr. G. P. Slade, the
well-known London solicitor (erstwhile of Sydney),
will be glad to hear that he is now recovering
from his long and severe illness —blood-poison-
ing after influenza. He will not come out un-
scathed, for he has lost the sight of one eye, and
is completely deaf in one ear.

a week extra for living at Coolgardie and the
Murchison.

Sweat is cheap in Melb. The sewing of
a pair of trousers is now done for 6d. Exclusive

Justice Hood objects to ordering the softer sex out of
Melb. Divorce-court when "shocking revelations" are
about to occur. “If women choose to come to the court
they know what to expect," says Judge Hood, and
assuming, no doubt, that it is the knowledge of what to
expect that attracts their fairy footsteps to the building,
he can see no reasonable excuse for denying them their
right to be present.
On last Day of Atonement in Sydney, a
hard-up man wanted to pawn his coat. After
tramping about for a couple of hours, and finding
all the pawnshops closed, he “g?,*e it best,” and
asked the first policeman he met when it was that
the pawnbrokers had decided upon taking a holi-
day, and where they were holding their picnic.

Melb. Age slobbers over the “great skill”
of a certain detective in tracing an alleged defaulter
to Colombo. The supposed skill of the much-
boomed one—beside whom, to read the local prints,
it would appear that Lecocq and Sherlock Holmes
were mere bunglers—consisted in making enquiries
of the van men employed to cart the Sitter’s furni-
ture from his house to the auction-room —an obvious
dodge employed by every tradesman in every
metropolis in the world to trace debtors.

Dear Bulletin,— A tight place. lam a
railway-porter, and was signalling a train into
the station-yard one night. I was standing on
the rails, just over a pair of points, when my foot
slipped between the points, and, no matter how I
tried, I could not get my foot loose. I could see
the train about half a mile away, and having no
lamp to show the red light, Igo ve myself up for
lost, when I heard a loud thumping, and there
were the engine and trucks off the line, not 10ft.
away !—Yours,
Teppoun, Q.

Mr. F. KENNA.
Perhaps the poems of Mr. Francis Kenna
(“K.,” of The Bulletin) are sweeter separately
savoured than collected in the neat little volume
just issued by Melville, Mullen, and Slade. His
pretty fancies are nearly always deftly phrased ;
yet, meeting them not as single spies, but as mar-
ried, with an offspring of 52 pages, one is assailed
by a sense of slightness, and sameness, and stiffness
all absent from the original reading. A set of
newspaper verses is a plea ; a book is a challenge ;
and we naturally extend to the debutant promise
a welcome which the veteran performance is
naturally expected to justify. Mr. Kenna’s
“ Songs of a Season ” is a collection of promises—
some of them very fine promises indeed ; but he
must do something better before he can be rank* d
with the immortals. Of course, he is not a
veteran yet; cr he would not jar a delicate ear by
rhyming “ dawn ” and “ newborn ’’—“dawning ”
and “ warning.” The Muse’s palate dislikes such
acids even in the hotch-potch of a hasty news-
paper ; and they are utterly forbidden to a poet
who sets out to scale Parnassus in nice brown
covers with a neat gold label. These sentences may
seem to read unkindly. They are not so meant,
for The Pulletin has real admiration and real
hopes for Mr. Kenna ; and, in reprinting a poem
which justifies both hope and admiration, it
assures readers who buy Mr. Kenna’s book that
they will find there two or three poems as good,
many nearly as good, and hardly one which doe*
not please to pause over.
“ AY, MARRY !” Shakespeare.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He’s weltered in Whitburn,
Simmered in Swinburne,
Maundered with Mackay,
The young Violin-Man.
Tussled with Tolstoi,
Idolised Ibsen,
Poems of Poe
Rattled his ribs on.
Screeched with their screech-
ing,
Struggled their strife,
Practised their preaching—
Hasn’t a wife.
Frenzied forehead,
Beard o’ bristles,
Pants horrid,
Worn to whistles.
Cuffs and collar
Running riot.
Hungry—holler—
Dreadful diet!
Buys a drink,
Swigs away,
Doesn’t think,
Forgets to pay.
Bleating 8.A.,
Sad and seedy,
M.A. someday,
Death—and d——d 1—
Go for glory 1
Ode Australia!
Write a story.
Fight a failure.
Chuck old Chaucer 1
Herrick —hang him !
Find a Fat Man,
Boomerang him.
Go to-morrow,
See M’Callum,
Buy—or borrow
Razor—alum.
Clear your chin
For amorous action.
Woo and win
A dear distraction:
Scattered rushes!
Gay carousal!
Blooms and blushes 1
Tender tousle !
Hearts attached,
Grief gone,
Pants patched.
Buttons on.
DOWELL O’REILLY.
HOW LONG, O GOD!
Tliiousin scopes of sky, and under
The deeps of earth «nd sea,
My soul went out in wonder,
To find hut myttery ;
A homeless storm-bird flying
Through dim dark seas uncrying,
Till spent with fruitless trying
It came again to me ;
To find its old home dreary.
Because all things had clanged.
And all old ways were weary,
For that its wings had ranged
O’er leagues of seas unsound, d,
Through skies by clouds unrounded,
Through fields of space unbounded.
In ways from wings estranged.
But ere its final turning,
It felt in frowning skies
The deathless glory burning
In Wisdom’s wond’rous eyes ;
That they who see, shall never
Find rest again for ever,
Till time at last shall sever
The grain from chaff of lies.
Mine eyes go forth to seaward
In mute expectancy,
To skyward, or to leeward,
To find some hope for me ;
The sullen skies are storm y,
And waves and winds before me ;
I curse the times that bore me,
And dream of times to be.
I have grown tired of living
A life that no one knows,
I sing no glad thanksgiving,
While men are weighed with woes ;
I know the winds are blowing,
I know the tide is flowing,
I know that Truth is growing,
Through all her thousand throes.
I know that Earth the mother
Shall be to all men free,
That each will owm the other
In days that yet shall be ;
I hear like maidens sirfging,
In leafy splendors swinging,
The slow years homeward bringing
The fruits of earth and saa.
But now 7, like sad seas sobbing
’Gainst bold, black headlands strong,
I hear the world’s woes throbbing
Against the shores of Wrong,
That shake above and under.
When Freedom’s tempests thunder—
I hear these things and wonder:
• “ How long, O God ! how long? ’’

Amy Roselle’s nightly demise, at Melb. Bijou, as Esther
Bandraz, makes the women snivel, but it is, for all that,
a rather stagey decease. She lies back on a sofa, in a
flood of limelight and a tight corset, and gasps like a
wheezy steam-roller doing a particularly uphill bit of
wdrk. Then she pants, tremolo, to the woman who has
been her hated rival, “ Ki-i-i-iss m-e-e a-g-gain, my
m-o-o-other ki-l-issed me so-o-o-o!” Having been duly
kissed, she rolls off the sofa into the very centre of the
stage, winks to the man who holds the curtain-rope in
the prompt corner, and gives up the ghost to slow music,
just as the sound of Dacre’s pistol comes from the wings
—Dacre having been suiciding in a leisurely fashion
during Esther’s last movements.
Ethel Haydon, of Melb., was engaged to support May
Yoh6 (now really Lady Clinton Hope, and presumptive
Duchess of Newcastle) in Sims and Caryll’s new piece,
“Little Dick Whittington,” at London Avenue. “I
almost pity her,” writes The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, “ for May brooks no rival near the throne, and
if the fair Ethel has really talent, and is likely to become
a favorite, the Hon. May will make it hot for her, as she
has for many others before —to wit, Alice Lethbridge, &c.
Frank Thornton’s daughter who_ was Savage Club
scholar at the Royal Academy of Music, is engaged to be
married to the son of Mr. Marler, a well-known London
estate-agent.
It transpires that a certain nobleman’s uncle journeyed
to Australia in the young man’s wake, with family in-
structions to use main force, if necessary, to prevent
the youth marrying an actress, or any other sort of
dreadful person.
An interesting theatrical elopement is reported from
up North. How any woman could ever run off from a
husband addicted to such magnificent ties is a mystery.

certain. Who was the Juggins?

in her dying scene. Neophytes can only achieve this kind
of thing by prayer and fasting.

Miss Roselle, accustomed to vociferous plaudits, is
quite alarmed at the stolidity of her Melb. audiences.
Nevertheless they enjoy her acting. But she dearly loves
a call.
Miss Roland Watts-Phillips is going to London. It may
not be generally known that Miss Phillips is the daughter
of the author of that splendidly poetical play “The Dead
Heart.”
Muriel Carr, the Dacres’ ingenue, is a literary Miss, and
writes for Jerumky Jerome’s magazine, “The Idler.” She
has the smallest hand on the stage.
Dear Bulletin, —It is a mistake to refer to Miss Hilda
Spong as a Home-grown and_ not an ’Orae-grown
a -tress. Miss Spong is English right through, and, with
ihe rest of her family, followed her talented father to
Australia after the production of “Little Jack Sheppard.”
Miss Hilda’s accent is sufficient proof of her English
origin.—McG.

A little girl with large, innocent eyes, baby curls, and
wearing a sunbonnet, fell into the hands of the Melb.
police the other day and told them a long, romantic story
Of a jouwipy from" Maoriland, and her experiences as a
homeless toddler in a strange, wicked city. She had
travelled to Melb., she said, to get an engagement as a
song-and-dance artist, and she had bounced accommoda-
tion at hotels for several days on the strength of being a
poor little thing without any ma. The sceptical police-
man immediately removed the sweet child’s sunbonnet,
whereupon she grew suddenly old and displayed an air of
much guile, and at once admitted that she wasn t an
orphan, nor was she of particularly tender years, and she
hailed from Brunswick, and was simply “out on her
own.” Case pending. The sunbonnet of late has covered
a multitude of sinners.
Paderewski, finding that his manager had arranged to
put him before a five-shilling audience at Torquay (Eng.),
refused to play the piano on those inferior terms. Ten
shillings per seat is the lowest price he will hear of.
For long Hare declined to “ lend ” Forbes Robertson to
Irving. Three times did the divine temptress, Ellen
Terry, try her witchery upon Robertson to get him to
beg Hare to spare him. At last came a note from the
much-wanted one to say that it was impossible, and that
Hare was obdurate. To this Ellen’s reply was a large
sheet of notepaper bearing but one letter-a big, big
Chicago D. Whereupon F.R. was at once lent.

After being without a theatre from the creation, Perth
(W.A.) is now to get two places of amusement. Modoy,
of the Hotel Metropole, is putting one up, and W. H.
Woodfield, (ate tenor robusto of W. & M.’s Opera Co., is
floating a £15,000 co. to build the other.
Haddon Chambers is working hard at a new melodrama.
Haddon’s collaborator is young Ralph Lumley, son-in-
law of Mrs. John Wood, who wrote, for his ma-in-law,
“Aunt Jack,” which proved such a success at the Court.
Cissy Loftus left the Augustin Daly Co. (so she says)
because she would not play second fiddle to Ada Rehan !
The real reason, says Tub Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, is that she is a dead frost as an actress, and so
has wisely reverted to the music-halls, where originality is
not quite so necessary. She has had an enormous suc-
cess in New York, and is coming on to London under
engagement to Morton, of the Palace, at a salary of £lOO
per week 1 How can the halls pay at such salaries?
Jenny Lee is going to play “ Jo ” in London once again.
Cissy Fitzgerald, the beauteous dancing damsel who
deserted the “Gaiety Girl” Company in New' York and
went over to a rival manager, has been legally restrained
from performing in America. George Edwards, who had
Cissy on a lease, obtained the injunction.

When Lydia Thompson horsewhipped Storey, editor of
Chicago Times, about 20 years ago, Bunyan, the magis-
trate who adjudicated upon the subsequent assault pro-
ceedings, dealt it out to Lydia unsparingly. The sou-
brette wrote Bunyan a nicely-scented, little pink note
acknowledging his impartiality, expressing her con-
trition for having, in a moment of excitement, committed
the assault, and enclosing four tickets for central reserved
front seats of the circle. Bunyan, who had a most unfor-
tunately-ornamented and highly-colored nose, fell into
the trap, attended the theatre en famille, and had the
satisfaction of hearing Lydia sing her (then famous) song,
“ It Ever I Cease to Love,” with the refrain at the end of
each verse altered to
“ May Banyan’s nose turn into a rose
If ever I cease to love !”
SPORTING NOTIONS.
From the long-drawn faces on Collina-street
Block after the result of the fifth test-match became
known, a stranger wou'd have thought that half the
population had died suddenly, and the other half was
mourning its loss.
* « • •
Great as was Turner’s disappointment at his
exclusion from the final test-team, it must have been
more than compensated for by Stoddart’s holding up the
ball at the conclusion of the match, and thanking the
gods that “ The Terror ” had not the handling of it.
• ♦ * •
The coolness of M‘Laren’s batting in the Eng-
lishmen’s second innings was painful—for Giff. There
were still 60 runs to be made when Mae. joined his county-
mate, Ward, who had got his own score to 70 and seemed
in a fair way to bring off a “century.” The new-comer
played as though they had thegarne already won, making
singles easily and lazily off Giffen for the sake of giving
Ward the next strike. However, the pro. didn’t make
the most of the opportunities offered him. A full-pitched
one from Harry Trott eventually took his wicket when he
was seven notches short of the hundred. Then Peel came
in, and the odd 20 runs required by England were
quickly knocked up.
Ward’s 93 was a very tame performance com-
pared with Brown’s 140. The little youngster demoralised
the Australian howling, while the cautious man at the other
wicket pottered along in very nervous style. Ward was a
constant reminder that the Englishmen had a heavy task
to struggle with, but Brown was a bold assertion that
Australia had better make up its mind to get licked. A
local hero, playing the game that Brown played, would
have been cheered at every stroke. Young Australia
only appreciates fast cricket from its own siie. The bulk
of the audience reserved its applause for the plucky bats-
man’s downfall.
M‘Kibbin was fated to chase the “fonrer ” that
won the test-match for the Englishmen. Having arrived
at the ball he was preparing to throw in as per usual, but
a hundred voices yelled, “ Keep it 1 ” Even then the
Bathurst pet seemed doubtful as to whether he had any
right to hold the ball, so they shouted again, “ Keep it! ”
A generous-minded bairacket for Australia moved as an
amendment—" Keep it, and give it to Brown.” He gave
it to Stoddart.
At the outset Stoddart’s team, on paper, had a
fair show of beating Australia, best three out of five, by
keeping the runs down. They left England with the
reputation of being' barely good enough to win on the
extra strength of their bowling. Yet it was the weakness
of the Australian attack which won them two victories in
Melbourne, and enabled them to snatch the first Sydney
game out of the fire.
The Englishmen’s six wickets to spare in last
week’s “decider” didn’t necessarily represent many'
runs, judging from the sick performance of the men who
came after M’Laren and Peel in the first innings. Ford,
Briggs and Lockwood batted so pooily under encouraging
circumstances, that no one would have felt surprised to
see them go for a “ duck ” apiece had they been required
to face a crisis. But England won by six wickets, and the
admitted unreliability of the tail-end of the team made
the batting work of the head and body so much the more
creditable. Furthermore, the Australians were outshone
by their rivals at every point. Richardson knocked down
6 wickets. Peel 3 and Briggs 2. Trott, who came out with
far the best average on our side, had only one case of
clean bowled. The three highest individual scores in the
match were made by M’Laren, Brown and Ward; also,
Peel got more runs for “ once out ” than any man on the
losing side. The Britishers’ fielding was a little better all-
round than the Australians’, and their rate of scoring was
the faster throughout the match.
» * » •
When the Englishmen come to meet Victoria
and South Australia, for a wind-up, they will drop the
careful game which Stoddart foisted upon them. Palience
was a virtue in the second test-match, but dash won
them the final. Stoddart himself hit almost as freely as
of old. Peel laid the wood on, M’Laren lost no time,
and Brown batted like a bantam-cock. Briggs, Ford, and
Lockwood opened lankily, and were promptly caught
close to the wicket. The only case of hitting that didn’t
succeed at all was Brockwell. He may “ come off ”
before the tour is finished.
» * » ♦
Stoddart, the most discreet of speechmakers,
having won the rubber, dropped a delicate reminder that
his team wasn’t the strongest All England lot imaginable,
though it had done what the strongest would have felt
proud to do. A seasonable remark considering all the local
references to “ ashes,” and “ match of the century.”
• • • •
Blackham must swallow his angry prophecy
that Richardson would get the cover knocked off him if
he came to Australia. They have scored off the express
bowler, but rarely punished him. It was the pace of the
ball that made most of the runs.

Two bits of cricket-report from Melb. Herald :
“Lyons went in to join Gregory after the Englishmen
had the good luelc to get rid of Darling by splendid field-
ing before he had well got going. Then Gregory, getting
in front of Richardson, nftluckrly just tipped one of the
express ones from the Surrey bowler behind the wickets.”
. . . “M‘Kd)binhad howling well, and trying to
get Brown in the slips, and at last he succeeded.” Bad
luck, it seems, killed the two Australians, but the
Britishers’ dismissal was the natural result of artful bowl-
ing. As a matter of fact, Brown went out of his way to
hit at the very “off ” M’Kibbin ball, because he had prac-
tically won the game and could afford to be incautious.
The ball never threatened his wicket. Ward subsequently
let half-a-dozen such pass him without attempting to
touch them.
« * ♦ •
When Johnny Briggs made his “duck ” in the
late test-match, he was never more disgusted in his life.
“Just like me,” he remarked candidly, “ the ball beat me
all the way, and I thought I was good for a hundred.”
Poor little Briggs.
♦ • ♦ ♦
It was stated in Wednesday’s papers that one
reason why Iredale did not score was his inability to sleep
the previous night, owing to a severe attack of toothache
and neuralgia. On reaching his hotel same night, he
found two telegrams from Sydney patent-medicine men,
advising him to try their tonics. In addition there were
sundry boxes of pills and liquid concoctions from Smell-
bourne vendors. Iredale was, of course, asked, in each
case, to send along a line as to the beneficial effects of the
mixtures.
* * ♦ *
The band was absent from the big match, and
the crowd felt grateful. It got tired of music that only
came without discord belw’een overs. During play the
performers’ eyes were divided between their own scores
and the other players’ scores, with a consequence that
“sundries,” “M’Laren, 100,” and the bowling-average
got plaved instead of the lovely strains of “Sweet Marie.”
The big drummer was the only consistent performer. He
was able to follow the game all the time, the only draw-
back being that he, in his cricket enthusiasm, always
kept on playing a solo some time after the rest had
knocked off.
* * * *
Overheard at the match : “ Who caught that?”
“Trott!” “What Trott?” “It’s not rot!” “Well,
what did you say it was for?” “ Because it was Trott !”
"What Trott?” “Rot to you, and be damned!”
“ Suna to you if you can’t answer a civil question !”
(Left thrashing the matter out).
