The Australian, defeat quite spoilt the big
“ continental ” arranged in Adelaide for Giffen. George
was to be welcomed as the captain and hero of the Aus-
tralian team but—alas, Brown and Ward !
That wizardess waa right. England won.
• « • •
In the five test-matches England played nine
innings and four wickets, and made 2399 runs : Australia
played nine innings and made 2822 runs. Players and
aggregate scores were as under :
England tried 7 bowlers in the five matches,
who bowled 5811 balls, 229 maidens, 4 wides, 7 no-balls
and took 86 wickets. Australia tried 14 bowlers, who
bowled 5682 balls, 283 maidens, 7 wides, 7 no-balls and
took 91 wickets. The Englishmen clean-bowled 33
wickets, of which Richardson took 26. The Australians
clean-bowled 28, Giffen being responsible for 9. Forty-
six Australians were caught out, Phillipson and Peel being
most successful with 8 each. Fifty-two Englishmen were
caught, Giffen, Iredale, H. Trott, Darling, and Bruce
dividing honors. Three Australians were stumped, 2 by
Phillipson, 1 by Gay, while 8 Englishmen were similarly
dismissed, Jarvis stumping 7. Four Australians w r ere run-
out, and 1 Englishman.
* * * ♦
The aggregate number of different players from
each cricketing province, and the runs contributed by
each group, in the five matches, were as follows
The sending of M'Kibbin to Brisbane was an
error in tactics. Whilst he was taking the Englishmen’s
wickets they took the measure of his twist.
* * * •
M’Kibbin’s bowling, as viewed by the English-
men, had no method in it. All twist and no length.
« * * *
As regards that fifth test-match, all that Aus-
tralia can congratulate itself upon is that H. Trott, the
fat man of the team, scored 42 runs in each innings,
obtained best bowling average against the English each
innings, and fielded with sensational adroitness and
success. This last consideration touches The Bulletin to
the core: imagination-fondly dwells upon the spectacle
of the fat man fielding with ideal grace and agility, and
covering the enemies of Australia with confusion amid
the thundeis of the assembled thousands. It compensates
for all. What though the battle between the teams was
bitter and prolonged ; what though Australia was ulti-
mately borne down and conquered ; what though Brown,
the fat man of the English, in great measure did it all
himself by scoring 140 runs at the crisis ! Did not Trott,
Australia’s fat man, shine in every department of the
game? And can anything be superior to all-round excel-
lence? And, therefore, though the laurels of the match as
such would otherwise go to the English team, Australia
can raise her head in pride, and gaze with love and ad-
miration upon her noble son, whose fat bore bravely up
upon that fatal day and wrested from the might "of a
whole conquering team the robe of triumph and the
crown of glory.
* * * ♦
Brockwell on Australian cricket: “The best
Australian wicket is far better than the best English
wicket, and the worst is much worse. . . . Events
have justified the opinion I formed that Bruce would be
the most dangerous batsmen we should meet on our tour.
. . . George Giffen stands perhaps without a peer as
an all-round player. . . . The Australian spectator is
apparently overstrung, and, as a consequence, is impatient
and intolerant. . . . The spectators would sometimes
get better play if, instead of badgering a player who has
for a moment failed, they would encourage him.”
» ♦ * *
Turner’s figures make him out to have been the
most valuable of the Australian bowlers in test-matches.
He certainly had the benefit of three wet wickets—two at
Sydney and one at Melbourne—to perform upon, but, on
the other hand, he stood out of the Adelaide match when
theover-anxious Englishmen were undoubtedly “cooked”
by the sun. Turner shifted 18 batsmen at an average
cost of 19£ runs, and his proportion of maiden overs was
40 per cent.
* » * *
Richardson’s batting figures are peculiar. In
test-matches he averaged 9 2-5 runs for 9 innings, although
his tallest score was only 12.
* * * •
Interest in the final test-match was specially
strong in Ballarat. When the result was announced at a
meeting of the local Exchange, an excited broker unfurled
a Union Jack and sang “ Kule Britannia,” which he
repeated later on in the share-market. This roused some
ot the Australians, who responded by hoisting the
Southern Cross and singing “ The Men of Australia.” A
ridiculous scene was brought to an appropriate conclusion
by the Englishers raising “ God save the Queen.”
» » ♦ »
Outside the Melb. Argus office the other Wed-
nesday morning some 1200 youths’ chests swelled so
enormously on learning of Sloddart’s downfall that the
crowd was pushed almost on to the tramlines. Three
hours later the same 1200 youths’ chests had shrunk so
small that you could have packed the lot into a tram-car.
* * * *
The swallows on Melb. Cricket Ground, when
skimming over the grass during the big match, deceived
many, who mistook them for the ball. People were taken
in so" often that they became annoyed, especially if they
had given a yell of applause for a boundary hit which had
been snapped up at point.
Just before the recent test-match, an M.C.C.
committee-man overheard, as he sat in his office, a sub-
ordinate promise an impecunious friend the loan of his
(the sub.’s) member’s ticket for the approaching match.
The listener said nothing then, but laid his plans so
effectually that, when the time came, not only was the
interloper refused admission, but his would-be benefactor
was hauled before the committee. More than one mem-
ber would have let the culprit off as a first offender,
but his accuser required a year’s suspension.
» ♦ * •
W. R. Wilson, having decided to get rid of his
stud and fixings, happens to win seven good races at
the big meeting. That is how the Devil always whispers
into a man’s ear-“ Wilson, you fool, your luck is turning.
Stick to it, old man 1”

Crown-street Public-school, has year after
year, owing to the presence of such players as Y.
Trumper, annexed the Sydney inter-school cricket badges.
At last it has suffered a fall—Woollahra, which has been
forging steadily ahead, having put it down rather badly.

Musidora, Mermaid, Briseis, First King, and Co. “Augur”
Chapman and a large party wine down lo see Don Juan,
about the Cup eve, for Wilson and Joe Thompson made
no bones about the big thing. Siys one: “He’s a

The raffling of the splendid St. Alban’s estate
and stud is probably only another pro< f that racing and
breeding combined don’t pay. Some 28 years ago the
Fishers di-poed of their big stud by art-union—the
draw took place at the old St. George’s Hall (now the Cam-
bridge Music Hall), Houike-st. The great Fishhook was
the first prize, and the children from the Blind School
worked the boxes. Johnny Wallis and Co., bookmakers,
drew Fishhook (and gave the blind girls £5O each), but he
was a failure, and at the stud got nothing—bar The Hook,
now in America.

smash : “lam an unlucky man ! I backed the wrong
horse all that day, and then 1 take a return ticket in this
blessed smash and never get a scratch.”

The Champion race lasted half a second longer
than the "full-stake ” limit time fixed by the V.R.O. —
srain. 45seo. Therefore, the owners of the winner and
placed horses received only half the money given last
year, and the sightseers, who had hoped to see a real three-
mile gallop, were glad to know the club saved £5OO by
the waiting tactics of the jockeys. Nothing started
off to make the running this year, for the simple reason
that none of the horses was reckoned equal to a big per-
formance. Dotty Patron is seemingly not quite the same
hero who won the Melbourne Cup, and Carnage, although
better than the Cup-winner at the present time, has not
recovered the “form ” which beat the Patron of sixteen
months ago. The Harvester was, no doubt, good enough
to win the race whatever way it had been run, but his
condition didn’t warrant an attempt to force the pace,
whilst Preston wasn’t sent to the front, inasmuch as Har-
vester had outstayed him in the Australian Cup two days
before.
Stevenson made a brilliant finish with Dampiei
for the V.R.O. Free Handicap. He came with a late rush
and won on the post, by half-a-head, in the English-
jockey style which Mick O’Brien cultivated so success-
fully here. But Stevenson’s bit of riding was merely the
desperate effort of a boy r who had been blocked until the
last minute. He would have rushed earlier—and might
have lost the race in consequence—but for being hampered
by the mob of cattle.
IT IS EASY TO ASK QUESTIONS.
A CHILD can ask questions that a wise man can’t
answer. Yes; and there are some questions that
the ablest doctors don’t like to have people ask
them. Say a question of this sort —while we are
about it, however, we might as well tell the story
straight away and have done with it.
Mrs. Sarah Mace was very ill with influenza.
That was in February, 1892, the time of the
epidemic. The attack was severe. She was
suddenly seized with a violent pain in the head
(both front and back) and temples. She had pain
in all her joints, too, and was hot and feverish.
She went to bed and sent for the doctor. He came,
examined her, took her temperature—over 100
degrees—and said very little, like a wise man.
The lady could not turn herself in bed. She lived
on slops, such as milk and broth ; she could not
swallow a morsel of solid food. Her husband
pressed her to take strong food, saying, “If you
don’t eat you will starve.” Right enough ; but he
forgot that when one can’t eat he can’t eat, and
there’s an end.
Well, she got weaker and weaker, and fell away
until there wasn’t much left of her but skin and
bone. Then, in her anxiety, she put her question :
“ Doctor, do you think I shall get well ?"
The doctor’s answer was true and honest; we
like and respect him for it. He said, “Mrs. Mace,
Ido not know.” He couldn’t tell. Neither could
any other doctor. No ; not even if he had a string
of titles to his name as long as a kite’s tail. But
did she get well? Wait a bit. We must hark
back a minute now.
Along about the 10th of March, 1880, Mrs. Mace
began to feel tired, languid and weary, as if her
work was too much for her, she said. She had a
bad taste in the mouth, poor appetite, and what-
ever she ate gave her pain at the chest and heart.
“I had,” she continues, “a dull heavy pain at the
right side and between the shoulders, and a sinking
feeling at the pit of the stomach, and a rising of
foul gas in my throat. Later on I had rheumatic
pains in my heart, chest and back. I sent for a
doctor, who attended me for six weeks, but I got
worse. One day the cook said, ‘Why don’t you
try Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup ? ’ —this being
a medicine my mistress (I was living with a lady
in Camden Town) kept in the house for family use.
I did so, and was soon as strong as ever.
Years fled away, and in 1891 she had the first
attack of influenza, and in 1892, the second, al-
ready described in part. We now complete the
account of the 1892 attack.
Mrs. Mace says: “The doctor continued to
attend mo for five weeks, and I took his medicines
for that time, but gained no strength. After hav-
ing suffered for seven weeks I said to my husband,
' I will now see what Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup will do for me. Perhaps it may cure
influenza as well as other ailments.’ I carried out
this resolution ; began taking the Syrup, and was
soon on my feet again, and have since kept in
excellent health, taking an occasional dose when
needed. (Signed) Sarah Mace, Monk’s Farm,
Great Warley, Brentwood, Essex, August 27th,
1892.”
Now we have never advertised Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup as a remedy for influenza. Yet
the facts stated by Mrs. Mace cannot be disputed.
What is the conclusion ? A very simple one in-
deed. She did not have influenza until her system
had been debilitated by indigestion and dyspepsia.
It is such people generally (almost wholly) that
all epidemic diseases attack. The dreaded cholera
scarcely ever touches anybody except a dyspeptic
—cholera is a malady of the bowels only. The
more we hear of disease the more the proof piles
up, showing that persons with a sound digestion
are safe against dying of anything except violence
or old age. The Syrup cured Mrs. Mace’s influenza
by taking away the ground it stood on —the tor-
pidity of her digestion.
She once asked, “ Doctor, do you think I shall
get well?” Time and Mother Seigel have said,
“Yes.”

In the matter of the application of Frederick Thomas Humphrey and Edward Percy Simpson, mort-
gagees under memorandum of mortgage from Alice Clara Mills, wife of John Yelverton Mills, No.
178,741, dated December 24, 1890, over the whole of the land comprised in Certificate of Title
vol. 7, folio 95; a second mortgage, No. 179,679, having been given to Messrs. James Norton and
Robert Smith.
WHEREAS the abovenamed mortgagees have made application for an order for foreclosure of the
mortgage above referred to, accompanied by such evidence of default for six months and other facts
as required by section 113 of the Real Property Act : Notice is hereby given, pursuant to the said Act'
that the land comprised within the said mortgage, as hereunder particularised, is offered and will
remain open for sale until 19th April, 1895, which date has been appointed as the time upon or
after which I, the Registrar-General, will issue to the applicants an order for foreclosure, which will
exclude all claim by the abovenamed mortgagor, and of any person claiming through or under her
as well as by the second mortgagees, unless in the interval a sufficient amount has been realised by the
sale of such land to satisfy the principal and interest moneys due, and all costs and expenses occasioned
by such proceedings, and interest up to the time of such sale.

Stevenson aforesaid, who won the Champion on
The Harvester, seems to be the coming light-weight. He
rode three other winners during the same afternoon,
showing splendid judgment of pace. He can scale 6st.
81b. without much trouble.
♦ » * •
Harvester’s defeat by Preston in the Leger is
now accounted for by lack of condition and the need of a
few “ winding-up ” gallops to make him “cherry-ripe.”
Touts had represented Cook’s favorite as the most wound-
up horse on the training-track.
* * * *
Both the V. R.C. and V.A.T.O. are to give a
farewell complimentary meeting to his Ex. Lord Hope-
toun, which doesn’t mean that his Ex. will scoop the pro-
ceeds. The compliment of being invited to come along
and see a lot of horses “run stiff” is rather a doubtful
one, but the business idea is brilliant. Williamson and
Musgrove may as well tender a grand farewell compli-
mentary matinee to Hopey, for their own benefit.
* * * *
When will the crowd decline to feed the Ring
by making favorites of good hors"B when they are really
owned by bookmakers? Surely two recent big events are
severe enough lessons for any but born idiots !
* * * *
Why should a hawker not be allowed to crawl
his rounds with a lame moke, whilst a racehorse owner is
allowed to try and cut out a Newmarket Handicap with a
dead lame ’un ?
* ♦ * *
According to the not very reliable local journals,
there was a capital attendance at Flemington on Cham-
pion Day, although the Australian Cup show, clashing
with the cricket-match, was admittedly a failure. The
writer can’t remember a smaller or tamer Champion Day
crowd than this year’s. Less than a dozen people saw the
favorite mounted in the paddock, and when Gough
jumped on to Carnage’s back, only the trainer and a
stable-boy were standing by. In the palmy days of White
and Hales, and even last year when Portsea left the pad-
dock for the course, a mob of admirers gave the favorite a
send-off. This year there was no send-off, not even an
“ ovation,” as the favorite galloped in a winner. Perhaps
the public are tired of horse-worship.
* * * *
Slavin (says The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent) has taken on a big job in tackling that smiling
darkey, “ Coffee-Cooler ” Craig. The “ Waler ” has just
gone into training and looks the picture of what a healthy
man should be six weeks before being wound up for a
stiff fight. He has a considerable advantage in height
and weight over his adversary, but the latter has never
yet been fairly extended, and we really don’t know how
good he is. Slavin we know all about, and as he has not
been exactly a Rechabite for the last two years, I have
very great doubt whether he will bear the strain. The
“Cooler,” on the contrary, is the personification of
steadiness—a man who, seeing that there is money in it,
makes use of his skill to take up a profession which he
really does not care about. He prudently invests his
winnings in good securities, and can now count his
wealth by thousands. [Cabled that Slavin knocked Craig
out in one round.]
* * * *
It was no disgrace, says The Bulletin’s London
correspondent, to Tom Williams, of Melbourne, to be
beaten by Dick Burge, who is not alone the champion in
his class, but one of the cleverest of boxers, and almost
Mitchell’s equal in ring-tactics. The blow which settled
Tom was not exactly a fluky one, but had the hurricane
fighter, as he is called, been a bit more careful he would
not have given the opening. He was just getting out of
reach when Burge saw his opportunity and almost threw
himself at Williams. They are sure to meet again, as
Williams’ backers still believe in the lad. Aldershot,
where Tom gives boxing lessons, was nearly “ broke ” by
his defeat.

Mr. B. E. Minns, an Australian-born
artist of sterling personal worth, whose name
and talent are familiar to Bulletin readers, and
whose portrait is printed on page 10 of this issue,
quits Sydney for England this week. The limits
of Australian art are still narrow, and there
naturally comes a time when our bush fledglings
yearn to try their pinions in a wider air. Mr.
Minns is of lusty growth, and goes with happy
auspices. He leaves behind him a tale of solid
artistic achievement which gives high promise for
his future. The Bulletin joins a host of friends
in wishing him the luck that his pluck and ability
deserve.
Cabled that Queensberry’s counsel, in the
Oscar Wilde libel-case, insinuates undue familiarity
between Oscar and the Marquis’ son—Lord Alfred
Douglas. Such charges are very easy to make,
and, once made, whether true or false, the man
accused, if at all sensitive, may as well commit
suicide.

John Duthie, M.H.R. (M.L.), who passed
through Sydney, the other day, en route for Eng-
land, where he proposes to slate the finance of
Premier Seddon’s Government —Treasurer Ward
is going ’Ome as a counterblast to Duthie —is a
large, grey Scotchman with a scowl and a re-
munerative wholesale and retail ironmongery
business situate in Wellington and adjacent parts.
Somebody once said of him he had “ murder in
his face and charity in his heart; ” and he is like
that. He has built up a large business in Maori-
land, has been Mayor and other things, is popular
with all classes, and has one of the finest stocks
of native obstinacy beneath the Southern Cross.
He is a slow, ponderous and determined speaker,
n > mean financial authority, and bites off the tails
of his words.
Mr. Guy Boothby, the well-advertised
and modest great Australian explorer, who knows
the “ secrets of the darkest portions of the con-
tinent” (he once performed an intrepid journey
from Brisbane overland to Maryborough, during
which the heroic party were lost in the scrub and
had to go without whisky for three whole hours),
is the son of Mr. Justice'Boothby, who years ago
caused such trouble by his “contumacy,” and was
removed from Adelaide Supreme Court Bench by
a vote of both Houses of Parliament. Mr. Guy
B. arrived in Adelaide five or six years ago, and
for two years was private secretary to Mr. L.
Cohen, money-lender, then Mayor of Adelaide —
who, in the eternal fitness of things, gave no less
than three balls during his term of office. While
in the confidential service of this much-respected
per-shenter Mr. Boothby must indeed have learned
a bit about “ the dark and mysterious places of the
great Australian continent.”
About 20 years ago an American darkey,
who was cooking for a station on Hellfire Plains,
having put together a cheque, started for the
nearest shanty to go into liquidation. He had a
high time for a week or so, and started away with
bluey, a bottle of supposed rum, and the horrors,
to seek for a new job and get a fresh calabash.
This was in, say, Jan., ’74. In Feb., ’75, a
man attached to a tank-sinking camp was
riding through the long grass, making for the
blue-ruin shop, when he came across a corpse,
black and perfectly preserved as to shape but as
hollow as a d.um. The clothes had been stripped
off and lay rotting not far away. The eyes were
open, and, strange to say, had not been eaten by
crows, cats, iguanas, or any other vermin. (The
want of timber accounts for this.) The empty
bottle lay beside the dead nigger. An inquest
was held, and the body ordered to be buried. A
coffin was made at the nearest station, and a
statien-hand sent to convey it to its destination.
The man stopped at the shanty to discuss things,
became cross-legged, and started off, perched on
top of the coffin, in the usual bush-pub. style— i.e.,
at top speed. Fell off, broke his back. A fresh
inquest was held, and a new coffin provided for
the nigger—which was sheer waste, as he didn’t
need one at all, and might very well have
been left in the long grass until the next bush-fire
came along. Doctor said the nig. was so well
pickled with rum or kerosene that he defied the
usual decaying influences, and gradually his
tissues were consumed by the ants, leaving bones
and skin, as may often be seen in the case of
horses and cattle. Does not Max O Rell say
that alcohol is King in this island ? It looks like
it, anyhow.— Gunyah Tom.
Melbourne monumental masons, if not
paid for their work, now pillory the dead and
shame the living by exhibiting in the front row of
their yards the tombstones, with inscriptions,
which have remained on their hands for the
reason aforesaid. Thu practice has grown con-
siderably during the lust two years.
The rumor of that legacy of £17,000, said
to have been willed to a famous boom-dame by her
long-lost unole, makes dead-and-buried financial
soandals “squeak and gibber” in their shrouds.
The fortunate legatee, ’tis said, leaves for England
shortly, to return later on with the dollars.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heelCC,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating whatsizeyou generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. It you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
count ry disl ricts where practical boofmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply Us patrons at the lowest cash price.

CITY MUTUAL LIFE
ASSURANCE SOCIETY, Limited.
HEAD OFFICE :
Intersection Hunter, Bligh & Castlereagh Streets, Sydney.
The most Liberal and Progressive Life Office in Australia.
IST Points worth noting about the 16th Annual lieport :—
1. NEW BUSINESS shows an Increase—£4l47, as against £3750
for 1893.
A Society’s increasing New Business is always Strong Evidence of its Increasing
Popularity.
2. RENEWAL PREMIUMS show a Considerable Improvement
—£22,593 for 1894, as against £21,293 for 1893.
The CITY MUTUAL LIFE is the Only Office which so far has been able to show an
Improvement in this respect.
3. DEATH CLAIMS are Remarkably Small. More than 40 per
cent, less than might have been expected under the Table
of Mortality on which the Society is working (the H.M.) —
£4983, as against £B5OO expected.
This evidences the Strictest Care in the Selection of Risks.
4. The BONUS CERTIEICATES for the past three years, 1892,
1893 and 1894, are expected to issue shortly. Alter this
Bonus Certificates will be Issued Annually.

St ALBAN’S ESTATE,

Conducted by Mr. George Adams (Tattersall’s).
IN placing this property before the public, Mr. Adams desires to say that no more bona
fide venture has ever been offered. Mr. W. R. Wilson has most reluctantly agreed to
submit it, owing to his time being entirely occupied with his other pursuits. In the history
of Australia no one has more consistently bought the best of everything, regardless of cost;
and his well-known and justly-deserved character for fair dealing is sufficient to dispel any
doubt as to the figures submitted, or the descriptions given of the various prizes.
As it is confidently expected the whole amount will be readily subscribed, early appli-
cation is necessary.
All overflow money received will be placed in the £lOO,OOO Consultation on next
Melbourne Cup, so that those outside the colonies need not hesitate about remitting in the
usual way. The Distribution will be drawn under Mr. Adams’ personal supervision, and
in the presence of anv subscribers and the public, at Brisbane, Queensland, as soon as it is
filled.

“Yes,” saida shipowner in conversation
with a passenger last week, “competition has
forced us to cut down passenger-rates all round
the coast, and every time we take a shilling off
you praise our public spirit and denounce other
companies that refuse to take a shilling off. Now,
why don’t you praise the other companies and
denounce us as blacklegs? When our men under-
cut that’s what you call them.”
In Westralia, the other day, a man out
back was relating to his blackboy the story of a
white N.S.W. bushman who, while timber-
splitting, got his hand jammed in a log, and how
awful evidence was left of the unavailing struggles
of the poor fellow, while dying of exhaustion, to
reach an axe, by which he might release himself.
The Westralian gave minute particulars to the
black savage, with the expectation that the latter
would evince some regret for the victim, or express
a wish that he had been on hand in time to avert
his fate. But what did the nigger say? Only
this: “By crikey, I s’pose that peller did
swear! ”
A sneak-thief fairly “diddled” a Melb.
policeman recently. The purloiner was just
emerging from a house with some flower-pots
under his arm when he ran into the officer who
enquired with a smile, “Phwat he was doin’
there?” With great presence of mind he replied
that he was taking his flower-pots to water at the
nearest horse-trough as his landlady would not
water them, and he had no time to do so as he was
studying all day. The guardian of the law walked
to the trough with him, walked back, and, as the
student walked up to the verandah, he went away
satisfied that it was all right. The sneak-thief
waited till he had gone, then put the door-mat
under his arm and cleared.

The acclimatised-fish question up to date.
Says Mr. Alex. Oliver, in S.M. Herald :
I do not think there is a Maoriland river that has not
been supplied with trout by the Acclimatisation Sociity,
which has been extremely generous and patriotic in
stocking their rivers. The principal trout they use is the
brown trout, and he gets an immense size, far larger than
in Britain, and is now becoming a salmon in his habits—
that is, he spends five-sixths of his time in the sea, which
is not what the brown trout does in Britain. Nine-tenths
of the trout in M.L. are the brown species. The
others are the “ rainbow trout,” and a few rivers
are stocked with the “brook trout,” and a few with
“Loch Leven trout.” It is about 100 to 1 when a man
catches a trout that it will be one of the brown variety.
They have grown so big that they have got beyond the
reach of the “ fly that is, the “fly” fisher cannot get
these fish, but the man with the artificial minnow can
catch them up to 201 b. From 121 b. to 151 b. are the com-
mon sizes to catch at the mouths of rivers and even
among the breakers. They are very often beautiful fish,
but, as a rule, the brown trout has not pink flesh, and is
“muddy.” In Lake Wakatipu they will not take bait,
and have to be caught by nets, but they are the best
tasting in M.L. These, also, are the brown trout. The
M.L. rivers have been so stocked with brown trout that it
is impossible to introduce any other, for the trout has
become cannibalistic, and devours all the weaker species.
The very curious fact is that the brown trout is evolving
himself into something that may become a salmon in
time. A good many scientific men are of opinion that it
may become a kind of M.L. salmon. As to salmon, they
have introduced many thousands of ova into the
likely rivers of the South Island, but the same
is true in regard to M.L. as in the case of
Tasmania. After leaving their nurseries the salmon
are not seen again. The fact is that these imported
salmon cannot live in salt water above 60 degrees,
and that temperature is, I think, probably fatal to the
salmon, so that when the fish gets down the rivers into
the “ parr ” state its instinct is to find salt water of a
temperature suitable to its development. Not finding it,
the fish goes south till it gets into currents or country
from which it never returns. No true salmon has ever
been found in Maoriland or Tasmania. The outcome of
my investigations is this : It is absolutely money wasted
to attempt to stock the Snowy River or any rivers of our-
with salmon ; but we may learn this lesson from Maoris
land-that if we want a big salmonoid the brown trout
will supply us with one.

column :
We ail stood around and knelt by the bed.

A Richmond, Yic., barber has invented a
bullet-proof material. When it comes to a choice
between holding his jaw or wearing a bullet-proof
under-shirt the barber does not hesitate.
Last Wednesday the facts about the Fijian
hurricane— which were known in Australia weeks
a g o —seem to have reached London, and the intel-
ligent cable-man promptly wired them back_ as
entirely fresh news. This is the sort of thing
which makes Australia fully appreciate the advan-
tages of having a cable. Some day an interesting
murder in Sydney will be cabled to London,
and the overwhelming intellect at the other end
will cable it all back again for fear the people at
Redfern and Woolloomooloo haven’t heard about
it.
Rev. Wm. Gray, missionary of Tanna, on
the Kanaka trade; —“ By Regulation 20 no
islander who has agreed to come to Queensland
and who afterwards leaves the ship can be made
to come back by force. This is how it is managed.
‘ When a boy jumps overboard just take a boat
and pull ahead of him, then lie between him and
the shore. If lie has not tired himself swimming
and passes the boat, keep on heading him in the
same way. This dodge rarely fails.’ (Recipe by
Oapt. ). The married couples (emigrating)
known to me, were, with one exception, elope-
ments. There is always a friend at hand to act
the ‘chief ’ and give consent. Like the Chinaman,
the Kanaka has an intense longing to be buried
at home. Eleven thousand islanders have died in
Queensland.”
The “good old times” have evidently
shifted to Westralia. At Perth a grog-merchant
sued a lawyer for <£6o worth of dishonored
cheques. The cheques had not come direct from
the lawyer, but through a lady, who, according to
plaintiff, kept a “ boarding-house,” where they
consumed as much as £55 worth of liquor in one
week. Some of the peroxided “young lady
boarders” ordered goods from this confiding
tradesman, who fulfilled the order and said in
Court that he “had no curiosity about Mrs. C.’s
business.” When defendant was in the box he
stated that he was drunk when he signed the
cheques, and that he was more or less drunk when,
on the advice of a friend, he went to try to get’
some of them back again. Then the hospitable
Mrs. 0. gave him more grog, and he faintly
remembered giving “Nellie” and “Minnie ’
cheques; then he became “silly and delirious.”
Altogether there are joyous times to the fore in
Perth.

The very latest swindle. A confiding
hayseed writes The Bulletin from a N.S.W.
country town :
Editor Bulletin, — Would you kindly oblige by letting
me know at your earliest convenience who is the com-
poser of the recitation entitled “The Burglar; or The
Ceckney Knobsman?” A professional actor named
, lately visiting this town, sold the right
to recite it to me for a certain sum of money. He also
told me that he composed it, and that your proprietary
bought the right to publish it, some little time back. The
recitation contains seven verses or 191 lim s. I am lead
to believe that he is not the composer, and, therefore, had
no right to sell it to me. The copy in my possession
bears the number (Ner. 247). Hoping you will he pleased
to give the informalion to me.—Yours, &c., W.T.D.
N.B.—When gave account of the recita-
tion he told me not to lose the copy. Afraid your paper
would prosecute me for reciting it as you bought the
right.
If the innocent Mr. W.T.D. can prove his state-
ments the police should be put on the track of
Mr. Blank Blank, who is quite unknown at this
s ffioe.

Mr. Airy Donnel, a squatter’s son, was coming
to town by train. At one of the stations a sharper
dropped in, and suggested 3d. Nap to kill time. The
game ran level till they were about half-an-hour from the
city, when the raagsman suggested higher stakes for the
final flatters. Airy agreed, and the stranger dealt him
ace, king, queen, jack of diamonds and ace of hearts,
reserving himself five diamonds, so as to pip Airy on the
last trick when he declared nap. The youth cried nap,
and offered to bet a sov. he would get through. Sharp
said he would lay a tenner he would not, and, after some
talk, it ended in £2O level being laid about it. Airy led
the ace of hearts; the other, of course, could not follow
suit, and the rest of his cards were too low to touch
Airy’s. The shark handed over the stakes and inquired
where the youth learnt the game. He explained that the
tutor on his father’s station was a graduate of a Scotch
university on the theological side.

The first Maori “ sport ” the present writer met
was a chieftainess of the Thames district, one Mary
Taipari. Mary’s husband was a mild old rangatira
who disapproved of horse-racing, and wore a billy-
hat and a frock-coat. But Mary had a strong
will and a private income, and she bought a roach-
backed stallion of uncertain age named Potentate.
First time he ran in Mary’s colors the fair owner climbed
on top of the judge’s box and performed an incantation,
supposed to have the virtue of imparting stamina and
speed, and then descended to put on her bit with a faith
that the result did not warrant, for poor old Potentate
finished nearer last than first. The jockey (a white)
didn’t weigh in —Mary didn’t give him a chance, for, sum-
moning some members of her tribe, she chased the
unhappy w retch up and down the course, and eventually
soothed her feelings by pulling out a handful of his
whiskers. ’
* • * *
The Southern civil-servant who has invented
the “revolutionary ’’ bicycle,” also lately got provisional
protection for an improved mud-guard that, in addition
to avoiding the old nuisance—the hosing of the rider with
mud—will have its V-shaped surface so presented that
the front guard will divide the air-currents during pro-
pulsion and the back guard will give the machine extra
velocity. The original machine built for trial purposes
has been broken up, and the sanguine inventor says he
would not take £300,000 cash down for his inventions.

crack player,” says a Bulletin correspondent, “ was, as a
rule, a demigod to whom the mad crowd bowed down.
To know and speak to the (treat man was an honor—l°
drink with hi.m a privilege reserved for none save the
illustrious. He drank and cursed and never worked, had
his buggy to take him to and from the ground, and his
man to dress and rub him down. As a rule he was a low-
looking ruffian with close-cropped head and a clean shave.
Yet one quiet-looking little man used to take the tram,
pay his fare, go down to the ground without any fuss,
lead his fourth year’s champions into the field, and take
no notice of the 2p,000 mad fools who roared themselves
hoarse. And when it was over the little man smiled at
the struggling hero-worshippers, went home, put on his
apron, sold His meat, and told the smallest barracker how
the big match was won. He played for the love of the
game, and it’s a pity he has retired. I allude to Alick
Dick, captain of the Essendon Football Club.” The Bul-
letin most respectfully salutes demigod Dick. It was
indeed most condescending for such a potentate not to
put his hat on with a shoe-horn.

respondent, seem to have easy times. About a dozen,
during Government time, make from £lO to £l2 books ©n

addition to this, a suburban hotel, in his wife’s name, has
the benefit of his enterprising management.

Hon, Bill Long- will auction his horses next
month, and race-goers, whom he has treated to so many
surprises in the past, are much exercised as to his future
turf-intentions. Anyhow, punters won’t go into mourn-
ing should he quite give up horse-owning." For the stiff-
hacked and wealthy William has always insisted that his
horses belonged strictly to himself, and chat anyone who
chose to hack one of his neddies did not thereby acquire
any sort of a partnership in the animal of his fancy.

♦ * * *
Summonses were served on two members of
the Victorian Club for betting on the stairs, and one
bookmaker w-as prosecuted. His counsel argued that
the Gaming Act was “simply intended to apply to persons
who were the owners, occupiers, or habitual frequenters
of premises for; the purposes of betting.” Clearly a
member of the Victorian Club is apart owner and habitual
frequenter for the purposes of betting, but the Bench dis-
missed the case after hearing a number of English prece-
dents. English precedents!
If a Melbourne bookmaker mustn’t ply his pencil
at any particular point of the V.C. premises, he’s
not entitled to bet at Flemington paddock. Just here
The Bulletin rises to ask why the V.R.C. or any other
racing-club is allowed to “ license ” bookmakers to bet
on the very ground where it can’t license a tote to do
the same thing? W'hy, in the name of moral decency, is
Parliament even asked for an opinion re the machine whilst
the human odds-iayer is a racecourse law unto himself?
And how would the law regard an enterprising Hebrew
who started to bet all fair, square and abovehoard, upon
the totalisator principle with a hook and pencil ? The
principle can be worked without a machine at all.
Then Sam Winter was prosecuted for publish-
ing, inMelb. Sportsman, “an advertisement setting forth
that a certain place known as the Victorian Club was
used for the purpose of making wagers.” Winter’s
counsel held that nobody on readinj the adet. could infer
that the club was used for purposes of betting. Then
bookmaker Jack Cohen was called to explain his advt.,
but Winter’s counsel objected to his evidence, and the
case was dismissed. Thus the farce proceeds from year
to year, the police (or Crown) pretending to have serious
intentions of asserting an insincere Law, and the Law
holding itself up to contempt. Thtsordlnary daily news-
paper report of overnight odds at the club doesn’t prove
that the place is a betting establishment. Not at all. As
far as the Law can understand, the club may be a house 6f
prayer.

The fiasco of the starting-machine at Caulfield
exactly carries out the prediction of Its enemies. Just
imagine the affair occurring over the Melb. Cup, with a
horse of Patron’s calibre left fresh to run the race over
again 1
• * * *
The false start at Caulfield could have been pre-
vented by the expedient of stationing a man with a red
flag about a furlong from the starting-post. If a false
start occurred, the man in charge of the machine w,ould
shake his red flag at the advance agent, who would then
stand on the track and wave the field back.

It would surprise some people if they knew how
few of the leather-lunged fraternity own the books they
make on a racecourse. It is an open racecourse secret
that at least one wealthy owner is “ at the back ” of one
of the biggest of “the books.”
• * ♦ •
There are “ millions in it ” if you are so lucky as
to have a decent connection. It is not so long since that,
in a Sydney Law Court, a small “book” swore that
when he retired he put his bag on another man, and
that his half share of the winnings of that book had
averaged £l4 weekly for the preceding year.
♦ * * ♦
One who should know declares that of all the
pmies racing around Sydney fully 90 per cent, get their
oats from the bookmakers and the tote-shops. Indeed,
one toteman is said to have as many gees in the bag as a
deceased bookmaker used to have peds. during the foot-
racing boom.

The action of the A. J.O. towards pony-racing,
a tew years ago, by driving good men out of the sport
and giving the toughs almost a monopoly, is responsible
for nine-tenths of the unspeakable villainy now associated
with the game.

The new football boot has a rubber sole with
projections for gripping the grass, a rubber shell heel,
and thick rubber toecap. The latter enables one to kick
straighter; it also makes a kick on the shin feel like a
blow with a sand-club.

They have a cup-race even at the Chatham
Islands, distance two miles, and a three-year-old named
Addington won it in a field of six. One would have
thought they could hardly have laid out a two-mile
course at the Ohathams except for a regatta.

Eitzsimmons : “Peter Jackson is not pretty,

Foxes are increasing rapidly in South Gipps-
land. The various Councils used to give £1 for every
“ brush ” produced ; now the reward is only 10s. Reynard
in winter lives almost solely on rabbits, but in the lamb-
ing season he destroys lambs wholesale.

A Melb. coloured pug. to an admiring circle :
“ Dis country’s rawten ; dat’s what it is, rawten ! If yeh
have a win, an’ a fellah wins a fiver, er a tenner, he don’t
know yeh-keeps away from yeh. If he loses fi’bawb,
dough, he soon find yeh, an’ let yeh know it, too. In
England if a man’s a gem-man an’ keeps hissef respeetable
he shakes hands wid lohds an’ docks ; an’ if dey have a
win dey'give yeh a hundred er two. Look at Pete Jack-
son !—shook hands wid de Prinoer Wales, an’ I’m as good
in my clawse as what Pete is in his. No, I tell yeh, dis
country’s rawten !”

THE “BOOKS.”
You chance to meet, in a Melbourne street,
A friend who does not smile
With mouth or eye, as he hurries by
In please-exouse-me style.
You give, perchance, one searching glance,
And note his troubled looks ;
On comes the crowd, as you muse aloud,
“ The ‘ books,’ no doubt, the ‘ books ’!”
On a steamboat bound for shores remote,
A man of “ sterling worth ”
You twig one day, ere it sails away,
About to find his berth ;
And he says, when press’t, he wants a rest
In peaceful foreign nooks,
Being far from strong. You know what’s wrong—
The “ books,” no doubt, the “ books ” I
Or young, or old, is the sloper bold,
Of high, or low, degree ;
In a moment rash he sneaks the cash
And slopes by land, or sea ;
Or p’raps for years, unthrilled by fears,
The balance-sheet he "cooks,”
Ere off he goes ; one eye we close—
“ The ‘ books,’ no doubt, the ‘ books ’!”

The Syren Sony.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Westward, westward, for ever westward,
The hurrying feet of the toilers pass ;
They will not wait in the flower-grown meadows,
They will not rest in the long lush grass,
But still press on for the arid deserts
’Neath skies that burn like a shield of brass.
Westward, westward, for ever westward,
Our loved ones leave us to join the throng;
They will not stay tho’ our arms are round them,
Their hearts are filled with the Syren song
That tells of gold in the lone land hidden
For saint or sinner, for weak or strong.
Westward, westward, for ever westward,
With fierce eyes fixed on tho phantom gold,
Although their feet through the hot sand passing
May tread on bones that are bleached and old :
The Syren Song is of those who conquer
And not of hearts that are still and cold.
Westward, westward, for ever westward,
The weird song floats on the burning air ;
It does not tell of the shining metals,
* It only whispers of long gold hair,
The sunny tresses of white-armed women !
The dead sea-fruit that is wondrous fair.
Westward, westward, for ever westward,
With words of promise like liquid fire,
That stir the blood ot the dreaming people
And speak to each of his heart’s desire,
Till sombre eyes are ablaze with passion,
And dead hopes rise from their funeral pyre.
Westward, westward, for ever westward,
The song floats by with its wild refrain ;
Madly the echoes mock, exulting
In ghastly tribute of heart and brain ;
But the Syren’s arms are for ever empty.
She sings—and then they are filled again.
Queensland.
ETHEL MILLS.
In My Theatre,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It costs only shoe-leather ; but the play goes on
continually and the artists are all stars. I have
spent hours watching this drama of the streets,
but the little girl on the step outside the fruit-shop
amused me best. She sold papers and—was the
most accomplished actress I have ever seen. The
electric-light shone full on her, and behind was a
mirror, so that both aspects of the poor, thin dress
could be seen at once.
I first noticed her having a row with a boy who
had been attempting to sell his papers on her
preserve-. Site drove him into the gutter and
across tiie street till he was glad to be allowed
the opposite corner, and he never poached again.
For whenever she chose to be angry her voice
and vocabulary were dreadful. But whenever she
chose to be otherwise—the same voice, with a
different vocabulary, was heart-breaking in its
pathos. And her eyes—naturally dimmed with
the taint of the streets—could melt pity and
pennies from the hardest cynic who once gazed
into them.
One evening an old gentleman stopped to speak
to her. He asked about her circumstances and was
very compassionate. There were not only the
peaked face and tender eyes to impress him, but
the pronounced shoulder-blades shivering in the
mirror behind as well.
“ Mother’s sick and I’ve two kiddies—young
sisters to keep, and father’s in the ’orsepital ”
“ My poor child !”
“ And then a poor g-y-url has no show, ’cos the
boys bullies yer so, and people don’t buy evenink
papers now like they used to. There was a time
when kind gentlemen, sir, used to gimme thrip-
pences for ’ERALps and let me keep the change,
but now .... Wot are ye givin’us ?”
“This ticket will admit you to an entertain-
ment in aid of the United Christian Endeavour
Association to-morrow night at eight o’clock, and
mind you ”
“ Strike-me-never !—and nothink else ?”
“There’s tea after the address, and the sisters
will take your name, and you will be entered on
the register.”
“Well, I’m blowed!—you bloomin’ fraud-
clear !”
“ My child !”
“ Ain’t yer child, and if I was I’d be ashamed
of me mother. Go on ; git out ! You’re interfer-
in’ with a respectable woman’s business.”
“But” —
“If you don’t skedaddle, ole Alleluia, I’ll give
yer in charge for insultin’ a female. Garn !”
He did—for he had heard of the flogging clauses
in the Crimes Act—while the girl repeated the
tale to the boy, who had come over to listen.
“ I thought I’d ’ooked a softy-softy, but ’e only
guv me this—rot ’im ! Look out, Billy, ’ere’s the
theatre push, and I hain’t done nothink to-night—
’Erald, sir—Won’t you buy the last one, sir—
’Erald, sir, ’Erald?”
« # •
Some time after this I was with a friend who
acts on a stage, and I said, “Come with me and
I’ll show you a better Camille than Bernhardt’s,”
and, while we were walking up the street, I told
him about my paper girl.
“ We’ll watch her from the other side—but no
—see !—she has started already and that’s her
latest.”
She was leaning back against a corner of the
wall in a wearied exhausted sleep, and we crossed
over.
“She can drop into it at a moment’s notice.
Look at the hang of that lip—isn’t it perfect!
Oh, I say, old man, keep a sympathetic face or
she’ll see it’s no good.”
Certainly it was perfect. The little head rest-
ing in the fluting of the pillar, the open palm
hanging limply over the edge of the step, were
very convincing.
“ Poor child, doesn’t she do it well ?” I said, in-
tending her to hear it, and so provoke her Billings-
gate.
My friend gently touched her.
“Lookout, old man-she has claws. Hallo!
she is asleep—catch her !”
“ You fool! She’s dead !”
Vic.

Mis Luck.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is a demi-monde of men as well as of
women, and the riches of romance are more
varied therein. The Devil is more interesting
than his dam.
Like the women, the men employ the whole
gamut of explanation. The prosperous boldly
declare that they chose their life because they
could see that it was the game that paid best. It
is only with the unsuccessful that explanation
turns to excuses. Then women and wine are
blamed, and the treachery of fellow-men; while the
philosophic account for everything by a compre-
hensive “ Kismet!”
The Big ’Un was a philosopher, and his name for
malignant Destiny was “My luck.” “It
was my luck that brought me to this !” he
would exclaim as he mechanically rigged invisible
thimbles on the table, or manipulated shadowy
cards. “ The year Sardius won the Ad’laide Cup.
Rec’lect? Well, I was a butcher then. Didn't
do a bad business, either.”
Then he would bite a quid from a cake of
tobacco and cogitatively roll it around in his
mouth. “One evenin',” he would resume, “a
pal of mine come into the shop with a scared look
on ’is face an’ ’e ses : ‘Big ’Un, the Kid’s gone an’
done it!’
“ ‘ Gone an’ done what ?’ ” ses I.
“ ‘ Why, robbed the bloomin’ bank,’ ses ’e.
“For a couple of jiffs I said nuthin’, but just
chewed over an idea that ’ad struck me. Then I
ses to Lawson—-my pal, you know —‘Just ’old on
a bit.’ Then I went into the back and come out
with a ’arf bottle of brandy. ‘Now,’ ses I, ‘ come
up-stairs.’ And up we goes. ‘Sit down!’ I ses,
‘ and ’ev’ a drink an’ listen to what I’ve got to
say.’
“We took a swig each. Then ses I, * Bob, this
is the chance of our life !’
“ ‘ What ?’ ’e ses, ‘ the Kid’s bit of snickin’ ?’
“ ‘ Yes !’ I ses, ‘ the Kid’s bit of snickin’. Just
you listen to me. We know, don’t we,’ I ses,
‘that Sardius is a cert, for the Cup. Well, the
Kid ’asn’t been dropt to yet, and ’e ’as only got
one chance.’
“ ‘ Well, spit it out,’ ses Bob.
“ ‘E must, ses I, ‘get old of all the stuff ’e can
an’ back Sardius. If ’e wins ’e can put every-
thing back. ‘lf ’e loses e’ won’t be any worse off
than ’e is now an’ can clear out.’
“ Bob said nothin’. ’E just shook my flipper
an’ took another brandy.
“ Well, to cut it short, the Cup was run a couple
of days after, an’ it was arranged that the Kid was
to meet me on the course with the stuff. I was
there to time, an’ ’e come up soon after.
“ ‘ Well,’ ses I, ‘ ’ev’ yer got the stuff ? ”
“ For answer ’e just clapped’s ’and on each of
’is pockets—’is trousers, ’is breast and’s side kicks.
They was ail bulged out.
“‘What are they?’ ses I, taken aback.
‘Fivers?’
“ ‘Tenners,’ he ses, ‘ an’ ponies ! ’
“ My Gord ! ’ ses I.
“Well, we met Bob a little after, and between
us got about four ’undred on our ’orse. We let
the Kid take the wagers ’cos ’e wasn’t known, an’
at the finish we stood to win about three thou.
“ When the race came on I was excited. My
oath ! Me an’ the Kid stood together in front of
the Stand, but Bob ’ung off with a petticoat ’e
’ad ’ooked onto.
“At last they started. I stood just be’ind the
Kid lookin’ over ’is ’ead an’ followin’ the race with
the glasses. Sardius was goin’ as good as could
be, an’ gettin’ a nice place at the turn, an’ the
crowd was singin’ out ‘Sardius! Sardius!’ ’E
come up the straight dancin’ in, an’ 1 saw that it
was all over, when suddenly the Kid falls in a
’cap at me feet, an’ in a couple of shakes two
blokes was on his chest.
“‘The cops!’ ses I to myself, an’ quietly
slipped away. I went to find Bob to tell ’im, but
couldn’t see ’im nowhere.
“My mind was in a state, my oath! because,
you see, the Kid ’ad all the tickets.”
At this point the Big Un would mop the per-
spiration from his forehead.
“ Well, I ’ung round for a bit an’ then thought
I would just quietly get back an’ see ’ow things
was goin’. I worked through cunnin’-like, an’
when I got to where we was standin’ what do yon
think I sees? Why, the bloomin’ Kid a-standin’
there as calm an’ unconcerned as if nothin’ ’ad
’appened.
“ ‘ Hello,’ I ses, ‘ didn’t yer get nicked ? ’
“ 1 Nicked ? No ! ’ ses ’e; ‘ I never.got nicked !’
“ ‘ Well, what was yer doin’ on the ground with
them two blokes on yer chest? ’ ses I.
“ ‘O, that? ’ ses he ; ‘ they said I ’ad a fit.’
“ ‘ You wouldn’t believe what a weight that took
off my mind,’ the Big ’Un would feelingly
remark while he wiped some more perspiration.
“‘Well, come on,’ I ses; ‘let’s go an’collect
the stuff. Where’s the tickets ?’
“’E put ’s ’ands in ’is kicks, first in one an’
then another. Then ’e looked in ’is ’at, an’ in the
end took off ’is boots an’ looked in them, but there
wasn’t no bloomin’ tickets anywhere. I got a bit
scared.
“ ‘ Ain’t yer got ’em?’ ses I.
“ ‘ I dunno,’ ses ’e, ’is voice shakin’.
“ ‘ Come on, now,’ I ses, suspectin’ funny busi-
ness ; no b y kid !
“ ‘ I ain’t kiddin, s’elp me Gord !’ ’e ses.
“ ‘Then where’s the tickets?’ I ses.
“‘ I dunno,’ ses ’e; ‘ the blokes must ’ave taken
’em when I was in the fit.’
“‘An’ so the ’ad,’ the Big ’Un would
conclude. ‘lt was just my luck!’”
* • #
As a sequel to this recital, he subsequently
added that he had found out the whole thing
was a game played off on him by the Kid and
“that Bob.”
“I might ’ave known,” he whimpered, “that
the little nipper wasn’t subject to fits. It was just
my luck!”
Melb.
